No Liability Without Driver and Exception for Hunting
Ruling on undriven dog: no liability, but hunting makes it halal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when an undriven dog causes harm, identify the exception for hunting, and explain the rationale based on the autonomy of the animal and necessity.

Core Idea
An animal's act is not attributed to its sender unless necessity requires it, as in hunting.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines a classic Hanafi distinction: when a dog is released without a driver, its harm does not impose liability on the sender. Yet if the same dog is released for hunting and kills prey, the prey becomes halal. How can the same action be treated differently? The answer lies in the nature of the animal's autonomy and the role of necessity (hājah). This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning (ta‘līl) behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) dog released without driver causing harm → no liability; (2) dog released for hunting without driver → prey is halal.
2
Understand the default rule
The default is that the animal is mukhtār (autonomous) and not a deputy (nā’ib), so its act is not attributed to the sender.
3
Recognize the exception
For hunting, necessity (hājah) overrides the default because hunting is only possible through the dog's action, and without attribution the prey would not be halal.
4
Apply the principle
Distinguish between cases where attribution is needed (hunting) and where it is not (harm/liability). The key is whether there is a need to transfer the act for legal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتا چھوڑا اور اس کا کوئی سائق نہیں تھا کوئی ضامن نہیں ہوگا
وكذا لو أرسل كلبا ولم يكن له سائقا لم يضمن ولو أرسله إلى صيد ولم يكن له سائقا فأخذ الصيد وقتله حل ووجه الفرق أن البهيمة مختارة فى فعلها ولا تصلح نائبة عن المرسل فلايضاف فعلها إلى غيرها هذا هو الحقيقة إلا أن الحاجة مست في الاصطياد فأضيف إلى المرسل لأن الاصطياد مشروع ولا طريق له سواه ولا حاجة في حق ضمان العدوان
ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر اس نے کتا چھوڑا اور وہ اس کا سائق نہیں تھا تو ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا اور اس کا سائق نہیں تھا۔ پس اس نے شکار پکڑ لیا اور اس کو قتل کر دیا تو حلال ہے، اور وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ چوپایہ اپنے فعل میں مختار ہے اور وہ مرسل کا نائب بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا تو چوپائے کا فعل اس کے غیر کی جانب مضاف نہ ہو گا۔ یہی حقیقت ہے مگر تحقیق حاجت پیش آتی ہے اصطیاد کی تو اصطیاد مرسل کی جانب مضاف ہوگا اس لئے کہ اصطیاد مباح ہے اور شکار کا اس کے علاوہ ( کہ کتے کا فعل مرسل کی جانب منسوب ہو ) کوئی طریقہ نہیں ہے اور تعدی کے ضمان کے حق میں کوئی حاجت نہیں ہے۔ تشریح اگر کتے کو چھوڑ دیا اور اس کو ہانکا نہیں اور اس نے کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا تو مرسل پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا ہو اور اس نے شکار کو پکڑ کر ہلاک کر دیا تو یہاں کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہوگا۔ اور ایسا سمجھیں گے گویا مرسل ہی نے شکار کو ذبح کر دیا ہے لہذا شکار حلال ہوگا۔

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵ سوال . اس کی کیا وجہ ہے کہ پہلی صورت میں کتنے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف نہیں ہوا اور دوسری صورت میں ہوا ہے؟ جواب حقیقت یہ ہے کہ کتا چوپایہ ہے جو اپنے فعل میں مختار ہے جو مرسل کا نائب ہونے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا، لہذا کتے کا فعل کسی دوسرے کی طرف مضاف بھی نہ ہونا چاہیئے۔
اس لئے پہلی صورت میں کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف نہیں ہوا۔ اور دوسری صورت میں مجبور کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہوا ہے۔
کیونکہ شکار مباح ہے اور شکار کی یہاں صرف یہی صورت ہے کہ کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہو جائے اور کتے کا مارنا پھاڑنا مرسل کے ذبیح کے درجہ میں ہو جائے ۔ ورنہ شکار حلال نہ ہو گا۔ خلاصہ کلام ... دوسری صورت میں مجبوری ہے اور پہلی صورت میں ضمان واجب کرنے کے لئے انتقال فعل کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
A dog is released and there is no driver for it; no one is liable. Similarly if he released a dog and he was not its driver, he is not liable; and if he released it for hunting and he was not its driver, and it took the prey and killed it, it is lawful. And the reason for the difference is that the quadruped is autonomous in its action and is not fit to be a deputy for the sender, so its act is not attributed to anyone else. This is the reality. But need arose in hunting, so it is attributed to the sender because hunting is permissible and there is no other way for it, and there is no need regarding liability for transgression. Explanation: If he released a dog and did not drive it, and it killed someone, liability is not obligatory upon the sender, because the dog's act is not attributed to the sender. And if he released the dog for hunting and it caught and killed the prey, here the dog's act is attributed to the sender, and it is as if the sender himself slaughtered the prey, so the prey is lawful. Question: What is the reason that in the first case the dog's act is not attributed to the sender, and in the second case it is? Answer: The reality is that the dog is a quadruped which is autonomous in its action, not fit to be a deputy for the sender, so the dog's act should not be attributed to another. Therefore in the first case the dog's act is not attributed to the sender. In the second case, due to necessity, the dog's act is attributed to the sender, because hunting is permissible and the only way is for the dog's act to be attributed to the sender and the dog's killing and tearing become like the sender's slaughter; otherwise the prey would not be lawful. Summary: In the second case there is necessity, and in the first case there is no need to transfer the act for imposing liability.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and its rationale: if a dog is released without a driver, the sender is not liable for any harm it causes, because the dog acts autonomously and its act is not attributed to the sender. However, an exception is made for hunting: even without a driver, if the dog catches and kills prey, the prey is lawful (halal) because the dog's act is considered as if the sender did the slaughter. The text explains this difference: in principle (haqīqah), the dog is autonomous and cannot be a deputy, but in hunting there is a practical necessity (hājah) to attribute the act to the sender, because hunting is permitted (mubāḥ) and there is no other way to make the prey lawful without this attribution. The commentary adds a question-answer clarifying that in the first case (harm) there is no need to transfer the act for liability, but in hunting there is a need for the prey to be halal. Thus, the same dog can have its act attributed or not depending on context.

Key Terms
مختار (mukhtār): Autonomous; the animal acts according to its own choice, not as a deputy of the sender.
حاجة (hājah): Necessity; a legitimate need that allows departure from the default rule (here, attributing the dog's act for hunting).
اصطياد (iṣṭiyād): Hunting; the act of catching and killing game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the prey considered halal when an undriven dog catches and kills it?
A) Because the dog is trained and always makes prey halal.
B) Because due to necessity, the dog's act is attributed to the sender as if he slaughtered.
C) Because the dog is not liable, so the prey is ownerless.
D) Because the dog kills instinctively and it is naturally permissible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an animal's act is not attributed to its owner unless necessity (hājah) requires it, as in hunting; for harm, the default rule of no liability applies.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Abu Yusuf's Precautionary Liability for Animal Damage
Intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf imposes liability in all animal damage cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for damage caused by animals and state why Imam Abu Yusuf's position became the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf imposes liability in all cases to protect property; his view becomes the fatwa.

This passage presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute about liability (ḍamān) for damage caused by animals. While the earlier established position distinguished between driven and undriven animals, Imam Abu Yusuf imposed liability in all cases as a precautionary measure to safeguard people's wealth. The passage concludes that the operative fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's view, illustrating how later Hanafi practice can adopt a dissenting opinion when it serves the Maqāṣid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier established position
The earlier ruling (attributed to Imam Muhammad or Abu Hanifa) differentiated between cases: if the animal was driven by its owner, the owner is liable; if not driven, there is no liability except in hunting contexts.
This shows the default Hanafi position before Abu Yusuf's refinement.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection and its basis
Abu Yusuf argued that liability should apply in all cases, regardless of driving, as a precaution (iḥtiyāṭ) and to protect people's property (ṣiyānat amwāl al-nās).
The principles of iḥtiyāṭ and ḥifẓ al-māl are used to expand liability.
3
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the earlier view, the owner of an undriven animal that causes damage would not be liable; under Abu Yusuf's view, the owner is always liable. This has significant implications for compensation.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the different outcome in specific cases.
4
Recognize the tarjih and its authority
The text states 'wa ʿalayh al-fatwā' — the fatwa is based on Abu Yusuf's view. This indicates that later Hanafi jurists elevated his position as the operative ruling for practical application.
This is a key example of how a dissenting intra-madhhab view can become the muftā bihī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر
وعن أبي يوسف أنه أوجب الضمان فى هذا كله احتياطا صيانة لأموال الناس ترجمہ اور ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ انہوں نے ان تمام صورتوں میں احتیاطاً ضمان واجب کیا ہے لوگوں کے اموال کی حفاظت کے لئے ۔
تشریح پہلی صورت میں سوق اور عدم سوق میں فرق کرتے ہوئے عدم سوق کی صورت میں ضمان واجب نہیں کیا گیا، لیکن امام ابو یوسف نے تمام صورتوں میں ضمان کو واجب کیا ہے تاکہ لوگوں کے اموال ضائع ہونے سے محفوظ رہ سکیں ۔ وعلیہ الفتوی۔
Imam Abu Yusuf's viewpoint: And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he made liability obligatory in all this as a precaution, safeguarding people's property. Translation: And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he made liability obligatory in all these cases as a precaution for the protection of people's wealth. Explanation: In the first case, distinguishing between with and without driving, liability was not made obligatory in the case without driving; but Imam Abu Yusuf made liability obligatory in all cases so that people's wealth may be protected from loss. And upon this is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute showing the position of Imam Abu Yusuf. While the earlier rulings distinguished between driven and undriven animals, and between harm and hunting, Abu Yusuf imposes liability (ḍamān) in all cases as a precautionary measure (ihtiyāṭ) to protect people's property. The commentary explains that unlike the first ruling which exempted the sender when there is no driver, Abu Yusuf's view is comprehensive: whenever an animal causes damage or kills property, the sender is liable regardless of driving. The text concludes with 'Wa alayhi al-fatwa', meaning the operative fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf's view.

Key Terms
احتياط (iḥtiyāṭ): Precaution; choosing the stricter ruling to ensure rights are protected.
صيانة (ṣiyānah): Preservation; protecting people's wealth from loss.
وعلیہ الفتوی (wa ʿalayh al-fatwā): Upon this is the fatwa; the relied-upon position for issuing legal verdicts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Yusuf impose liability in all cases according to the text?
A) Because animals are always under owner's control.
B) As a precaution to protect people's property.
C) Because the dog is always a deputy.
D) Because driving is irrelevant in all cases.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the muftā bihī may follow a dissenting imam's view when precaution and public interest demand it, as seen in Abu Yusuf's position on animal liability.

Liability for a Beast Released on a Public Road
Ruling on liability for beast released in public road

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sender is liable for harm caused by a released beast on a public road and identify the exception that extends liability.

Core Idea
Liability for a released beast requires immediate harm on its straight course; deviation or stop severs liability unless no alternative path.

This passage from al-Mabsut, one of the core books of Zahir al-Riwayah, lays out a key rule of tort liability (ḍamān) in Hanafi fiqh. It illustrates how causation is determined through the concept of 'attribution' (ḍiyāfah): the sender is responsible for the beast's movement only as long as it remains on its natural, straight course. Understanding this rule helps you apply the principle that liability is tied to direct and unbroken causation, with a clear exception when the beast has no other path to follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is releasing a beast on a public road. The ruling: the sender is liable (ḍamān wājib) if the beast immediately (fawran) causes harm while going straight.
Liability is not absolute; it depends on the beast's behavior.
2
Locate the key condition
The condition is that the beast goes 'on its natural course' (ʿalā sananihā) — that is, straight ahead without deviation.
The term 'sanan' refers to the beast's customary way of walking.
3
Apply the exception
If the beast turns right/left or stops and then resumes, the ruling of release is severed (inqaṭaʿa ḥukm al-irsāl), unless the beast has no alternative path.
The exception prevents injustice when the road is narrow.
4
Trace the reasoning (taʿlīl)
The reasoning: the beast's walking is attributed to the sender (māḍāf ilayh) only while it continues on its straight course. Deviation breaks the causal link.
This is a form of causation analysis based on custom (ʿurf).
5
Note the practical nuance
Timing is crucial: the harm must occur immediately (fawr) after release. If delayed, the release may not be the cause.
The word 'fawr' indicates uninterrupted sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے چوپایہ چھوڑا اس نے فوراً کسی پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل ضامن ہے
قال رضى الله عنه وذكر فى المبسوط إذا أرسل دابة فى طريق المسلمين فأصابت في فورها فالمرسل ضامن لأن سير ها مضاف إليه ما دامت تسير على سننها ولو انعطفت يمنة أو يسرة انقطع حكم الإرسال إلا إذا لم يكن له طریق آخر سواه وكذا إذا وقـــــفت ثـــــم ســـــارت
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور مبسوط میں مذکور ہے کہ جب کسی نے مسلمانوں کے راستہ میں چوپایہ چھوڑا پس اس نے فوراً کسی پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل ضامن ہے اس لئے کہ اس کا چلنا مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہے جب تک کہ وہ اپنی روش پر چلتا رہے گا اور اگر دائیں یا بائیں مڑ گیا تو ارسال کا حکم منقطع ہو گیا مگر جب کہ اس کے لئے اس کے علاوہ کوئی راستہ نہ ہو، اور ایسے ہی جب کہ وہ کھڑا ہو جائے پھر چلے۔
تشریح کسی نے راستہ میں اپنا کوئی چوپایہ چھوڑ دیا اور وہ ابھی اپنی سابق روش پر چل رہے ہیں اور اسی اثناء میں اس نے کسی پر حملہ کر کے اس کو ہلاک کر دیا تو مرسل پر ضمان واجب ہوگا اور اگر اس نے روش بدل دی یعنی راستہ کشادہ ہے سیدھا چلنے میں کوئی دقت نہیں اس کے باوجود وہ راستہ میں دائیں بائیں مڑا، یا کھڑا ہو گیا اور پھر چلا تو سابق روش ختم ہو جانے کی وجہ سے ارسال کا حکم منقطع ہو گیا اور اب
Someone released a beast; it immediately attacked someone, so the sender is liable. He (author) said, may Allah be pleased with him, and it is mentioned in al-Mabsut: If he released a beast in the path of Muslims and it struck immediately, the sender is liable because its walking is attributed to him as long as it goes on its (natural) course. If it turns right or left, the ruling of release is severed, unless it has no other path besides that. Similarly if it stopped then walked. Translation: The author said, and it is mentioned in al-Mabsut: When someone released a beast in the path of Muslims and it immediately attacked someone, the sender is liable because its walking is attributed to him as long as it continues on its (straight) course. If it turns right or left, the ruling of release is severed, unless it has no other path besides that. And similarly if it stopped and then walked. Explanation: Someone released his beast on the road, and it was still going on its previous course, and during that it attacked and killed someone, then liability is obligatory upon the sender. If it changed its course—i.e., the road is wide and there is no obstruction in going straight, yet it turned right or left, or stopped and then walked—then due to the ending of the previous course, the ruling of release is severed, and now...
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Mabsut (one of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah) about liability for a beast released in a public road. The key rule: if the beast causes harm immediately while still going straight on its natural course ('alā sananihā), the sender is liable because the beast's movement is attributed to him. However, if the beast deviates by turning right or left (when the road is wide and there is no necessity), or if it stops and then walks again, the liability ceases because the ruling of the release (ḥukm al-irsāl) is terminated. An exception is if the beast has no other path; then even turning does not sever attribution. The commentary explains that as long as the beast continues on its straight path, the sender is liable; if it deviates or stops, the original release is no longer the cause of the harm.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): Immediately; without delay. The harm must occur right after release while the beast is still on its initial course.
سير على سننها (sayr ʿalā sananihā): Going on its natural or customary course without deviation.
انقطع حكم الإرسال (inqaṭaʿa ḥukm al-irsāl): The ruling of the release is severed or terminated, meaning the sender is no longer liable for the beast's actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mabsut, when does the sender's liability cease after releasing a beast on the road?
A) When the beast kills someone.
B) When the beast turns right or left or stops, unless no other path.
C) When the beast reaches its destination.
D) When the owner calls it back.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on beast liability hinges on causation: the beast's straight course immediately after release.

Liability for a Released Dog: The Hanafi Principle and Abu Yusuf's Dissent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for a released dog

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when a dog's action is attributed to its sender and explain why Imam Abu Yusuf made liability wajib in all cases as a precaution.

Core Idea
A dog is an independent actor; its action is attributed to the sender only when necessary for a lawful purpose like hunting.

This passage addresses a key question in Hanafi diyat: when is a person liable for the harm caused by an animal they release? The earlier Hanafi position holds that a dog is an independent actor, so its actions are not normally attributed to the sender. However, in hunting, attribution is necessary for the kill to be lawful. Imam Abu Yusuf disagreed, imposing liability in all cases to protect people's wealth. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind both views and explains why the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
What is the precise issue: under what conditions is a person liable for harm caused by a dog they sent?
2
Understand the earlier position's reasoning
The dog is an independent agent; its action is not normally attributed to the sender. Only in hunting, where the kill must be treated as the sender's slaughter, does necessity compel attribution.
This establishes a default rule: no attribution unless required by a lawful purpose.
3
Contrast Imam Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf makes liability wajib in all scenarios as a precaution (ihtiyatan) to protect people's wealth, regardless of attribution.
His reasoning is based on public welfare (hifz al-amwal).
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In the earlier view, if a dog sent for non-hunting attacks someone, the sender is not liable. Under Abu Yusuf, he is always liable.
5
Note the tarjih (preference)
The text states 'wa alayhi al-fatwa' — the fatwa follows Imam Abu Yusuf's position.
This reflects a shift towards precaution in later Hanafi practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات
۲۴۴
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵ سوال . اس کی کیا وجہ ہے کہ پہلی صورت میں کتنے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف نہیں ہوا اور دوسری صورت میں ہوا ہے؟ جواب حقیقت یہ ہے کہ کتا چوپایہ ہے جو اپنے فعل میں مختار ہے جو مرسل کا نائب ہونے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا، لہذا کتے کا فعل کسی دوسرے کی طرف مضاف بھی نہ ہونا چاہیئے۔
اس لئے پہلی صورت میں کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف نہیں ہوا۔ اور دوسری صورت میں مجبور کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہوا ہے۔
کیونکہ شکار مباح ہے اور شکار کی یہاں صرف یہی صورت ہے کہ کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہو جائے اور کتے کا مار نا پھاڑ نا ، مرسل کے ذبیح کے درجہ میں ہو جائے ۔ ورنہ شکار حلال نہ ہو گا۔ خلاصہ کلام ... دوسری صورت میں مجبوری ہے اور پہلی صورت میں ضمان واجب کرنے کے لئے انتقال فعل کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر
وعن أبي يوسف أنه أوجب الضمان فى هذا كله احتياطا صيانة لأموال الناس ترجمہ اور ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ انہوں نے ان تمام صورتوں میں احتیاطاً ضمان واجب کیا ہے لوگوں کے اموال کی حفاظت كے لئے ۔
تشریح پہلی صورت میں سوق اور عدم سوق میں فرق کرتے ہوئے عدم سوق کی صورت میں سیمان واجب نہیں کیا گیا، لیکن امام ابو یوسف نے تمام صورتوں میں ضمان کو واجب کیا ہے تاکہ لوگوں کے اموال ضائع ہونے سے محفوظ رہ سکیں ۔ وعلیہ الفتوی۔
Chapter of blood money. 244. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 15. Question: What is the reason that in the first scenario the dog's action is not attributed to the sender and in the second scenario it is attributed? Answer: The reality is that the dog is an animal that is independent in its action and does not have the capacity to be the deputy of the sender; therefore the dog's action should not be attributed to anyone else. For this reason, in the first scenario the dog's action is not attributed to the sender. And in the second scenario, the dog's compelled action is attributed to the sender. Because hunting is permissible and the only way for hunting to be lawful here is that the dog's action is attributed to the sender, and the dog's killing and tearing become like the sender's slaughter; otherwise hunting would not be lawful. Summary: In the second scenario there is compulsion, and in the first scenario there is no need for transfer of action to impose liability. Imam Abu Yusuf's viewpoint: And from Abu Yusuf that he imposed liability in all of this as a precaution to protect people's wealth. Translation: It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he made liability obligatory in all these cases as a precaution for the protection of people's wealth. Explanation: In the first scenario, distinguishing between market and non-market, in the non-market case liability was not imposed, but Imam Abu Yusuf made liability obligatory in all cases so that people's wealth may be protected from loss. And upon this is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal attribution of an animal's harmful act to its sender (mursil). It begins with a question about why in one scenario the dog's action is not attributed to the sender while in another it is attributed. The answer explains that a dog is an independent actor, not a mere tool, so its action is not normally attributed to the sender. However, in hunting, because hunting is only lawful if the dog's kill is treated as the sender's slaughter, the dog's action is attributed to the sender out of necessity. This establishes a principle: attribution occurs only when necessary for a lawful purpose. The passage then introduces Imam Abu Yusuf's dissenting view: he imposes liability (daman) in all such cases as a precautionary measure to protect people's property, regardless of whether the action is attributed. The text notes that the fatwa is given on Abu Yusuf's position, reflecting a preference for public welfare over strict causality.

Key Terms
مرسل (mursil): The person who sends or releases an animal (dog, horse, etc.) and is potentially liable for its actions.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation, especially for damages caused by one's animal to person or property.
تسبیب (tasbib): Causation through indirect means; a legal basis for liability when one sets in motion a chain of events leading to harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the earlier Hanafi position attribute the dog's action to the sender in hunting but not in other scenarios?
A) Because the dog is trained to hunt.
B) Because hunting is only lawful if the dog's kill is treated as the sender's slaughter.
C) Because the dog is considered a deputy in hunting.
D) Because the sender accompanies the dog in hunting.

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Yusuf's position, that a sender is liable for all harm caused by a released dog, is the operative fatwa in the Hanafi school, overriding the earlier view that required attribution of the action.

Liability for Animal Released on the Road
Ruling on liability when an animal immediately harms someone after being released on the road.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sender of an animal is liable for harm caused on a public road according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish when liability is cut off.

Core Idea
The sender is liable only if the animal harms immediately on its natural course; voluntary deviation cuts liability.

This lesson examines a ruling from al-Mabsut on diyat (liability) when an animal released on a public road causes harm. The key principle is that liability hinges on whether the animal's movement is directly attributable to the sender's act of release. The ruling draws a clear line: immediate harm on the animal's natural course implicates the sender, but any voluntary deviation or stop on a wide road severs that liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is releasing a quadruped on a public road. The ruling: if the animal immediately harms someone while proceeding on its natural course, the sender is liable (wajib daman).
2
Locate the dalil
The source is al-Mabsut, a Zahir al-Riwayah text, quoting the statement: 'When a person releases an animal in the path of Muslims and it immediately strikes (someone), the sender is liable because its movement is attributed to him as long as it proceeds on its course.'
The dalil is based on the attribution of the animal's movement to the sender.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'as long as it proceeds on its course' (ما دامت تسير على سننها) is the operative legal indicator. It means liability is tied to the animal's movement being a direct continuation of the release. The animal's sayr (movement) is legally attributed to the sender as long as it follows its natural path.
If the animal deviates or stops, the attribution ends.
4
Note the exception
If the road is so narrow that the animal cannot proceed without turning, then any deviation is still considered part of its original course, and liability remains. This is an exception to the general rule.
The condition is 'unless there is no other path for it'.
5
Determine when liability ends
Liability ends if the animal voluntarily turns right or left on a wide road, or stops and then moves again. In such cases, the animal's movement is no longer attributed to the sender.
The text states: 'if it turns right or left, the rule of release is cut off' and 'similarly, if it stops and then moves'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے چوپایہ چھوڑا اس نے فوراً کسی پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل ضامن ہے
قال رضى الله عنه وذكر فى المبسوط إذا أرسل دابة فى طريق المسلمين فأصابت في فورها فالمرسل ضامن لأن سير ها مضاف إليه ما دامت تسير على سننها ولو انعطفت يمنة أو يسرة انقطع حكم الإرسال إلا إذا لم يكن له طریق آخر سواه وكذا إذا وقفـــــــت ثـــــم ســــــــارت
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور مبسوط میں مذکور ہے کہ جب کسی نے مسلمانوں کے راستہ میں چوپایہ چھوڑا پس اس نے فوراً کسی پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل ضامین ہے اس لئے کہ اس کا چلنا مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہے جب تک کہ وہ اپنی روش پر چلتا رہے گا اور اگر دائیں یا بائیں مڑ گیا تو ارسال کا حکم منقطع ہو گیا مگر جب کہ اس کے لئے اس کے علاوہ کوئی راستہ نہ ہو، اور ایسے ہی جب کہ وہ کھڑا ہو جائے پھر چلے۔ تشریح کسی نے راستہ میں اپنا کوئی چوپایہ چھوڑ دیا اور وہ ابھی اپنی سابق روش پر چل رہے ہیں اور اسی اثناء میں اس نے کسی پر حملہ کر کے اس کو ہلاک کر دیا تو مرسل پر ضمان واجب ہوگا اور اگر اس نے روش بدل دی یعنی راستہ کشادہ ہے سیدھا چلنے میں کوئی دقت نہیں اس کے باوجود وہ راستہ میں دائیں بائیں مڑا، یا کھڑا ہو گیا اور پھر چلا تو سابق روش ختم ہو جانے کہ وجہ سے ارسال کا حکم منقطع ہو گیا اور اب مرسل پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔
البتہ اگر راستہ تنگ ہو کہ بغیر مرے کڑے اس میں چلنا ممکن نہ ہو تو اس کی سابق روش پر سمجھا جائے گا اور مرسل پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔
Someone released a quadruped and it immediately attacked someone, so the sender is liable. The author said: It is mentioned in al-Mabsut: When a person releases an animal in the path of Muslims and it immediately strikes (someone), the sender is liable because its movement is attributed to him as long as it proceeds on its course. And if it turns right or left, the rule of release is cut off, unless there is no other path for it. Similarly, if it stops and then moves. Translation: The author said: It is mentioned in al-Mabsut: When a person releases an animal in the path of Muslims and it immediately strikes someone, the sender is liable because its movement is attributed to him as long as it proceeds on its course. And if it turns right or left, the rule of release is cut off, unless there is no other path for it. Similarly, if it stops and then moves. Explanation: A person released his quadruped in the road and it is still going on its original course, and in the meantime it attacked and killed someone, then the sender is liable. And if it changed its course, i.e., the road is spacious and there is no difficulty in going straight, yet it turned right or left, or stopped and then moved, then due to the end of the original course, the rule of release is cut off and now the sender is not liable. However, if the road is narrow such that moving without turning is impossible, then it is considered as on its original course and the sender is liable.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Mabsut about liability for an animal released on a public road. The key principle is that the sender is liable if the animal causes harm immediately while proceeding on its natural course (sanna). The attribution of the animal's movement to the sender (mursil) continues as long as the animal follows its original direction. If the animal voluntarily turns or stops, the sender's liability is cut off because the animal deviated from the attributed path. However, an exception is made when the road is so narrow that turning is unavoidable; in that case, any deviation is still considered part of the original course. The commentary emphasizes that the ruling hinges on whether the animal's action can be deemed a continuation of the sender's act of release.

Key Terms
ارسال (irsal): The act of releasing or sending an animal, which creates a legal relationship of potential liability for its actions.
سیر (sayr): The movement or gait of the animal; when it proceeds on its natural course, the movement is attributed to the sender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the sender's liability for an animal released on the road cease according to al-Mabsut?
A) When the animal stops and then moves again.
B) When the animal turns right or left only if the road is narrow.
C) When the animal deviates from its original course voluntarily on a wide road.
D) When the animal attacks after a long period.

Key Takeaway
The sender's liability for a released animal depends on whether the animal's harm occurred immediately on its natural, unbroken course; any voluntary deviation on a wide road severs that liability.

When Does a Pause Cut the Sender's Liability? Hunting Dog vs. Horse
Distinguishes purpose‑serving from purpose‑contradictory pauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of maqsud (purpose) to determine whether an animal's pause after release terminates the sender's liability.

Core Idea
A pause that serves the sender's purpose does not cut liability; a pause that contradicts it does.

Trained hunting dogs and horses are sent by their owners for specific purposes. When they pause mid‑action, does the owner remain liable for their subsequent acts? The Hanafi school looks to the owner's intention: if the pause helps achieve that purpose, liability continues; if it hinders, liability ends. This rule applies in diyat and other liability cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and the sender's purpose
Determine why the sender released the animal: for hunting (dog) or for travel (horse). The purpose (maqsud) is the key.
2
Evaluate the pause against the purpose
Does the pause help or hinder the purpose? For a hunting dog, a pause to reposition aids hunting; for a horse, a pause stops travel.
3
Apply the rule
If the pause serves the purpose, irsal continues and the sender remains liable. If it contradicts the purpose, irsal is terminated.
4
Distinguish the two examples
Dog: pause is for tactical advantage (purpose‑serving) → liability continues. Horse: pause is for resting (purpose‑contradictory) → liability cuts off.
5
Generalise the principle
The same analysis applies to any animal or even inanimate object (e.g., a drone) sent for a purpose: evaluate whether the pause aligns with that purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف ما إذا وقفت بعد الإرسال فى الاصطياد ثم سارت فأخذت الصيد لأن تلك الوقفة تحقق مقصود المرسل لأنه لتمكنه من الصيد وهذه تنافى مقصود المرسل وهو السير فينقطع حكم الإرسال ترجمه بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ وہ چوپایہ (یعنی کلب معلم ) کھڑا ہو گیا ہو شکار پر چھوڑنے کے بعد پھر چلا ہو پس اس نے شکار پکڑا ہو تو ارسال منقطع نہ ہوگا ) اس لئے کہ یہ ٹھہر نا مرسل کے مقصود کو ثابت کرتا ہے اس لئے کہ یہ ٹھہرنا اس کے شکار پر قابو پانے کے لئے ہے اور یہ ( چوپایہ کا مثلاً گھوڑے کا ٹھہر نامرسیل کے مقصود کے منافی ہے اور مقصود چلنا ہے پس ارسال کا حکم منقطع ہو جائے گا۔ تشریح اگر کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا اور وہ کھڑا ہو گیا اور پھر چلا تو ارسال ختم نہیں ہوا۔ اور شکار حلال ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ یہ اس لئے ٹھہرا ہے تا کہ شکار پکڑنے کا داؤ لگائے اور یہی مرسل کا مقصود ہے اور گھوڑے وغیرہ میں مرسل کا مقصود سیر ہے اور جب وہ کھڑا ہو گیا تو مرسل کا مقصد ہونے کی وجہ سے ارسال منقطع ہو جائے گا۔
Contrary to the case when it stops after being sent for hunting, then goes and catches the prey, because that pause actualizes the sender's purpose, since it is for enabling it to catch the prey; while this (stopping of a horse) contradicts the sender's purpose which is movement, so the rule of sending is cut off. Translation: Contrary to the case when the animal (i.e., a trained dog) stops after being released for hunting, then moves and catches the prey, then the sending is not cut off. Because this pause confirms the sender's purpose, since this pause is to enable it to overpower the prey. And this (e.g., a horse's stopping) contradicts the sender's purpose, and the purpose is movement, so the rule of sending is cut off. Explanation: If a dog is released for hunting and it stops and then moves, the sending is not terminated, and the hunt is lawful. Because it stops to maneuver to catch the prey, and that is precisely the sender's purpose. But in the case of a horse, the sender's purpose is movement, and when it stops, the purpose is contradicted, so the sending is cut off.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuance: whether an animal's pause after release cuts off the sender's liability. The key is the sender's intent. For a hunting dog, a pause (e.g., to position for attack) actually serves the sender's purpose of catching prey, so the attribution continues. For a horse sent to travel, stopping contradicts the purpose of movement, so irsal is terminated. This distinction is based on the object of the release: hunting versus travel.

Key Terms
مقصود (maqsud): The purpose or objective of the sender when releasing the animal; used to determine whether the animal's pause is consistent with liability.
کلب معلم (kalb mu'allam): A trained hunting dog; its actions are more likely to be attributed to the sender due to training.
ارسال (irsal): The act of sending an animal with a specific intent; its continuity depends on whether the animal's actions align with the sender's purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a hunting dog's pause not terminate the sender's liability while a horse's pause does?
A) Because a dog is trained and a horse is not.
B) Because the dog's pause serves the purpose of hunting, while the horse's pause contradicts the purpose of travel.
C) Because a dog is an animal of prey and a horse is not.
D) Because the sender explicitly allows the dog to pause.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school evaluates pauses based on the sender's purpose: if the pause serves that purpose, liability continues; if it contradicts, it ends.

Liability for Harm by Animal: The Role of Ta'addi
Contrasts liability for harm by an animal based on the legality of the act of sending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when liability for harm caused by an animal is wajib versus not wajib based on the presence of ta'addi in the act of sending.

Core Idea
Liability for indirect harm requires transgression; a permissible act like hunting does not generate liability.

This passage explores a key principle in Hanafi tort law: whether the owner of an animal is liable for harm it causes depends on the legality of the act that set the harm in motion. The text contrasts two scenarios — sending a dog to hunt versus releasing it on a public road — to illustrate that transgression (ta'addi) is the essential element for liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two contrasting cases: sending a dog for hunting vs. sending it on a road. Identify the ruling for each case (liable / not liable).
2
Locate the operative criterion
The passage states that liability depends on whether the act of sending involves transgression (ta'addi). Identify the specific words that express this cause.
Look for 'tعدی' and 'مباح'.
3
Analyse the reasoning (talil)
Understand why hunting is mubah and road obstruction is ta'addi. Then derive the legal maxim: 'Indirect causation (tasbib) only creates liability when the original act is transgressive.'
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a case where a person trains a falcon for hunting and it accidentally harms someone. Using the same principle, determine liability.
Falconry is also mubah, so no liability.
5
Note the cross-madhhab perspective
Other schools may not require ta'addi for all cases. Recognise the Hanafi position as one valid view.
The text sets the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبخلاف ما إذا أرسله إلى صيد فأصابت نفسا أو مالا في فوره لا يضمنه من أرسله وفي الإرسال في الطريق يضمنه لأن شغل الطريق تعد فيضمن ما تولد منه أما الإرسال للاصطياد فمباح ولا تسبيب إلا بوصف التعدى ترجمہ اور بخلاف اس صورت کے جب کہ کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑ اہو پس اس نے فورا کسی نفس یا مال پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل اس کا ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ اور راستہ میں چھوڑنے کی وجہ سے ضامن ہو گا اس لئے کہ راستہ کو مشغول کرنا تعدی ہے تو اس شغل سے جو اثر ظاہر ہو گا اس کا ضامن ہوگا۔ بہر حال ارسال شکار کے لئے مباح ہے اور نہیں تسبیب (یعنی وہ تسبیب جو مہمان کا باعث ہو ) مگر تعدی کی صفت کے ساتھ ۔ تشریح کسی نے اپنا کتا شکار پر چھوڑا اور اس نے فورا کسی انسان یا مال کو ہلاک کر دیا تو یہاں مرسل پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا کیونکہ شکار مباح ہے تو اس سبب میں تعدی نہیں پائی گئی۔ اور سبب اسی وقت موجب ضمان ہوتا ہے جب اس میں تعدی ہو۔ اور اگر اس نے راستہ میں چھوڑ دیا ہو اور اس نے مذکورہ حرکت کی تو مرسل ضامن ہوگا کیونکہ راستہ میں چھوڑ نا تعدی ہے۔
And contrary to the case when he sent it (the dog) to hunt, and it immediately struck a person or property, the sender is not liable; and in the case of sending on the road, he is liable because occupying the road is transgression, so he is liable for whatever results from that occupation. As for sending for hunting, it is permissible, and there is no indirect causation except with the attribute of transgression. Translation: And contrary to the case when a dog is sent to hunt and it immediately attacks a person or property, the sender is not liable for it. And due to sending on the road, he is liable because occupying the road is transgression, so he is liable for the effect that appears from that occupation. In any case, sending for hunting is permissible, and there is no indirect causation (i.e., causation that entails liability) except with the attribute of transgression. Explanation: Someone sent his dog for hunting and it immediately killed a person or property, then the sender is not liable here because hunting is permissible, so there is no transgression in this cause. And a cause only gives rise to liability when there is transgression in it. And if he had sent it on the road and it did the mentioned action, then the sender is liable because sending on the road is transgression.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This final passage clarifies the principle that liability for indirect harm (tasbib) requires an element of transgression (ta'addi). Sending a dog for hunting is a permissible act (mubah), so if the dog unexpectedly harms a person or property, the sender is not liable because the act itself is not tortious. In contrast, releasing an animal on a public road is a transgression (since it obstructs the way), so any harm resulting from that transgression triggers liability. This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi tort law: the legality of the underlying act determines whether indirect causation creates liability.

Key Terms
تعدی (ta'addi): Transgression or wrongdoing; the necessary condition for indirect causation (tasbib) to give rise to liability.
مباح (mubah): Permissible act; performing it does not create liability for unintended consequences unless there is transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a person not liable when his dog, sent for hunting, harms a human or property?
A) Because the dog is trained and not under control.
B) Because hunting is permissible and there is no transgression in the act of sending.
C) Because the person did not intend to harm.
D) Because the dog is considered a wild animal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, liability for indirect harm requires transgression (ta'addi) in the causative act; a permissible act like hunting does not create liability.

Liability for Damage by a Released Animal
Ruling on owner liability when released animal instantly damages crops

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a releaser is liable for damage caused by an animal, and apply the hadith 'جرح العجماء جبار' to determine liability.

Core Idea
Owner liability for animal damage requires direct causal link: release with immediate harm, not escape.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies Hanafi ruling on liability when a released animal causes damage. The key distinction is between immediate, uninterrupted damage after release and later deviation or escape. The hadith 'جرح العجماء جبار' sets the default: no liability for damage by an escaped animal. Understanding the causal connection is essential for applying this ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between immediate damage after release (no deviation) and damage after deviation or escape.
The passage also mentions simple escape regardless of time.
2
Determine liability in each case
Releaser liable only for immediate damage; no liability for deviation or escape.
Liability requires causal link via owner's action.
3
Find the proof
The hadith 'جرح العجماء جبار' and the principle of attribution (lack of release, driving etc.).
Hadith is used as a general exemption.
4
Understand the causal link
Liability requires an action from the owner (release) that directly leads to harm; if the animal deviates or escapes, the link is broken.
Similar to driving, leading, riding.
5
Apply the rule to similar cases
Consider if the owner was driving, leading, or riding the animal – different rules apply.
Those are not detailed here but mentioned.

He said: And if he released an animal and it immediately destroyed crops, the releaser is liable. But if it turned right or left while there was another path for it, he is not liable, due to what has passed. And if the animal escaped and hit property or a person, night or day, there is no liability on its owner, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The wound of the dumb animal is inconsequential.' Muhammad said: It is the escaped one. And because the action is not attributed to him due to the absence of what necessitates attribution to him, such as releasing and its like. Translation: The author said: If he released an animal and it immediately ruined a crop, the releaser is liable. And if it turned right or left while there was another path, he is not liable, due to the same evidence that passed. And if the animal escaped and killed some property or a person, night or day, then there is no liability on its owner, due to the command of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The wound of the four-legged beast is inconsequential.' And Muhammad said: The one that is called 'dumb' in the hadith is the one that escapes. And because the action is not attributed to the owner due to the absence of that which necessitates attribution, like releasing and its likes (driving, leading, riding). Commentary: It has passed earlier that after release, if the animal deviates from its prior course, the release is severed. Therefore, if the owner released an animal and it immediately ruined someone's crop, the releaser is liable. But if it deviated from its course and then ruined the crop, no liability is due. If the animal itself escaped and fled and killed a person or someone's property, night or day, no liability falls on the owner due to the mentioned hadith. Also, in this case, no action from the owner is found by which the animal's action can be attributed to him, meaning there is neither release, nor driving, nor leading, nor riding from the owner.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This is a ruling on liability for damage caused by an animal after the owner has released it. The passage distinguishes between immediate damage (where the animal follows its normal course) and delayed damage (where the animal deviates). The key principle is that liability attaches only when the owner's action (release) is directly connected to the harm. If the animal later strays, the causal link is broken. Additionally, if the animal escapes on its own (without any intentional release), the owner is not liable at all, based on the prophetic hadith 'جرح العجماء جبار' (the wound of the dumb animal is of no consequence). The commentator explains that 'عجماء' refers to a loose, untended animal. The ruling applies both day and night, and the owner's lack of direct action (such as releasing, driving, or riding) prevents attribution of the harm to him.

Key Terms
عجماء (ʿajmāʾ): The text defines it (via Muhammad) as the animal that escapes on its own (المنفلتة).
جبار (jubbār): Meaning 'inconsequential' or 'without liability'; the injury caused by such an animal is not compensated.
إرسال (irsāl): The act of releasing or letting loose an animal intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the animal causes any damage after release.
B) Only if the animal damages immediately after release without deviating.
C) Only if the animal escapes at night.
D) Only if the owner was riding the animal.

Key Takeaway
The owner is liable for animal damage only when his own action (release) directly causes immediate harm; once the animal deviates or escapes independently, liability falls away.

Compensation for a Goat's Eye: Nuqsan Only
Ruling that compensation for a goat's eye is only the decrease in market value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the compensation for injuring a goat's eye and explain why it is measured by the decrease in market value (nuqṣān) rather than a fixed share.

Core Idea
For a meat animal, compensation for injury is only the loss in market value, not a fixed fraction.

This passage addresses a specific case: a butcher's goat has its eye gouged out. The ruling establishes that the compensation (ḍamān) is not a predetermined share of the animal's value but the actual decrease in market price. The reasoning is that the goat's primary purpose is meat, so the eye damage only affects its sale price. This lesson will walk through the legal logic and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Find the specific ruling in the text: compensation is the decrease in value (nuqṣān). The act is gouging out the eye of a goat.
2
Identify the underlying principle
Understand the rationale: because the goat's purpose is meat (مقصود هو اللحم), only the market value loss matters.
3
Confirm the scope of the ruling
The mention of 'butcher' is incidental; the ruling applies to any goat, and similarly to other meat animals (cows, camels) as will be clarified later.
4
Contrast with what it is not
This ruling rejects a fixed share (like 1/4) for eye injury. Compensation is the actual decrease in sale price, not a fraction of the animal's value.

Source Text

A goat belonging to a butcher: its eye was gouged out. In it is what diminishes its value, because the intended purpose from it is meat, so only the decrease is considered. Translation: A butcher's goat, its eye was gouged; so in it is the amount of the deficiency that occurred, because the intended purpose from it is meat, so only the meat is considered. Commentary: Someone gouged out someone's goat's eye. Since the goat's purpose is meat, here only the deficiency is obligatory. (And the definition of deficiency is known.) The restriction to butcher is incidental; a goat, regardless of whose it is, has the same ruling. Similarly, a cow, camel, etc., regardless of whose they are, have the same ruling that will come later.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This is a ruling on compensation (ḍamān) for injuring a goat by gouging out its eye. The text establishes that the compensation is not a fixed share (like a quarter of the value) but rather the actual decrease in market value of the goat. The reasoning is that the primary purpose of a goat is meat; thus the eye does not affect the meat, so only the reduction in sale price is due. The commentator notes that the mention of a 'butcher' is incidental—the ruling applies to any goat. This is contrasted with animals like cows and camels, which have additional purposes (work, riding), and their ruling will be different (as stated in the next passage).

Key Terms
نقصان (nuqṣān): The difference in market value of the animal before and after the injury.
قصاب (qaṣṣāb): A butcher; the context indicates that the animal is intended for meat, but the ruling is not limited to butchers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The amount by which the goat's market value decreased.

Key Takeaway
For goats (and similar meat animals), compensation for a non-fatal injury is the actual loss in market value, not a fixed share.

Compensation for Eye of Work Animals: One-Quarter Value vs. Decrease in Value
Ruling and dispute on eye compensation for work animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for eye compensation of work animals, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument based on analogy to goats, and explain the Hanafi rational proof based on dual resemblance.

Core Idea
For work animals, eye compensation is fixed at one-quarter value.”

This passage addresses the compensation for gouging out the eye of animals such as cows, camels, horses, donkeys, and mules. Unlike goats, which are purely for meat, these work animals serve multiple purposes, leading the Hanafi school to fix the compensation at one-quarter of the animal's value. Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, applying the general rule of decrease in value by analogy to goats. The Hanafi response combines a hadith with a rational argument based on the animal's resemblance to humans in function and to edible animals in consumption.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: compensation for one eye of a work animal (cow, camel, horse, etc.) is one-quarter of its market value.
This is a fixed fraction, not a case-by-case assessment.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that compensation is only the decrease in the animal's value, analogizing to the case of a goat.
Shafi'i treats all animals uniformly as property.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i dalil is qiyas: the goat is an animal with only meat value; any eye injury reduces its value proportionally. By analogy, all animals should follow this rule.
This treats animals as generic property.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) a hadith and ruling of Umar specifying one-quarter for a dābba, overriding the general analogy. (2) A rational argument: work animals resemble humans in having multiple functions (work, beauty) so they deserve a fixed fraction, but they also resemble edible animals, so the fraction is set at one-quarter, not one-half.
The dual resemblance (shabahayn) method is a form of istihsan.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
For a camel worth $1000, Hanafi awards $250; Shafi'i awards only the actual decrease in market value (e.g., $150). The practical difference is significant.
The fixed fraction provides predictability.

And in the eye of a butcher's cow and his camel is one-quarter of the value, and likewise in the eye of a donkey, mule, and horse. Al-Shafi'i said: In it also is the deficiency, by analogy with the goat. Our proof is what is narrated that he (peace be upon him) judged for the eye of a riding beast one-quarter of the value, and likewise Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) judged. Translation: And for the eye of a butcher's cow and his camel, one-quarter of the value is due, and likewise for the eye of a donkey, mule, and horse. Imam Shafi'i said that in it also is the deficiency, analogizing to the goat. And our proof is what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged for the eye of a beast one-quarter of the value, and likewise Umar judged. Commentary: If instead of a goat, the eye of a camel or cow etc. was gouged, then in that case compensation of one-quarter of its value is obligatory. Imam Shafi'i said here also what is the ruling for a goat, i.e., payment of the deficiency is obligatory. We have taken proof from the judgment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the judgment of Umar. And because in them (cow and camel etc.) there are many purposes besides meat, such as carrying loads, riding, adornment, beauty, and work. From this aspect they resemble humans. And they are also kept for eating, so from that aspect they resemble edible things. So we acted on both resemblances: by the resemblance to humans we made one-quarter obligatory, and by the other resemblance we negated one-half. And because it is possible to perform work with them by four eyes: its two eyes and the two eyes of the user, so it is as if it has four eyes. Therefore, due to the loss of one of them, one-quarter of the value is obligatory. Commentary: Why is one-quarter of the value obligatory for the eye of a camel, cow etc.? This is the rational proof of the principle. Its summary is that the purpose of a goat is only meat, but these quadrupeds are not only for meat; they have many other purposes: they are used for ploughing, transporting goods, adornment and beauty. Due to these functions, they have some resemblance to humans, and they are also eaten, so they resemble edible things. So it is appropriate to act on both resemblances: due to resemblance to humans, we made 1/4 of the value obligatory.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling for compensation for gouging out the eye of work animals (cow, camel, donkey, mule, horse). Unlike the goat (which is only for meat), these animals have multiple purposes: labor, riding, beauty. The compensation is fixed at one-quarter of the animal's value. Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, holding that compensation should be only the decrease in value, by analogy with the goat. The Hanafi school provides two proofs: (1) a narration that the Prophet (ﷺ) and Caliph Umar (رضي الله عنه) judged one-quarter of the value for a riding beast (dābba), and (2) a rational argument: these animals are like humans in having multiple functions (eyes for work) but also like edible animals, so the compensation is set between the full diya for a human (where one eye would be half the value) and the minimal decrease for a purely edible animal. The four-eye analogy (the animal's two eyes plus the user's two eyes) yields one-quarter per eye lost.

Key Terms
ربع القيمة (rubʿ al-qīmah): One-quarter of the animal's market value; the fixed compensation for the eye of work animals.
دابة (dābba): A riding or work animal, such as a horse, mule, donkey, or camel used for transport.
شبه (shabah): Resemblance; used in the rational proof to analogize animals to humans (for work) and to edible animals (for meat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary textual proof for the Hanafi ruling on eye compensation for work animals?
A) A Quranic verse
B) A hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) and the ruling of Umar
C) Consensus (ijma)
D) Analogy to goats

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school fixes compensation for the eye of work animals at one-quarter of their value, based on a prophetic judgment and a dual-resemblance rational argument distinguishing them from purely meat animals.

Diyat — Blood Money
beginner
5 min
Basic Ruling: Liability of the Goader for Animal-Caused Death
Statement of ruling with companion narration as proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the goader (al-nākhiṣ) is liable for death caused by an animal he goaded, and identify the textual evidence from the Companions.

Core Idea
The goader, not the rider, bears liability for an animal's deadly reaction — his transgression imputes the act to him.

When an animal is goaded and causes death, who pays blood money? This lesson examines the core ruling from al-Hidayah, supported by the practice of 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. We will see why the goader is considered the direct cause and the rider is presumed innocent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Imagine a rider on an animal in the road; another person goads the animal, causing it to kick, strike, or shy and kill someone.
The animal's reaction can be any harmful motion directly caused by the goading.
2
State the ruling
The liability (daman) for the death is on the goader (al-nākhiṣ), not the rider (al-rākib).
This includes blood money (diyah) payable by the goader's 'āqilah.
3
Identify the textual proof
The ruling is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them) as a practice of the Companions.
This is considered a qawl sahabi, authoritative in Hanafi fiqh though not from Quran or mutawatir Sunnah.
4
Recognize the scope
The ruling applies to various animal reactions: kicking, striking with forelegs, shying and colliding, jumping, or trampling.

Book of Blood Money. 248. Ashraf al-Bidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya Volume 15. ... (previous passage on eye injury). A person was sitting on his animal, another goaded it, causing it to become startled and kill someone. The liability is on the goader. He said: Whoever travels on an animal in the road, and a man strikes it or goads it, and it kicks a person, or strikes him with its forelegs, or shies and collides with him and kills him, that is upon the goader, not the rider. This is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with them. Translation: The author said: And whoever travels on an animal in the road, then a person struck that animal or goaded it, and the animal kicked a person, or struck him with its forelegs, or shied and collided with him and killed him, then liability is on the goader, not the rider. This is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. Explanation: Zaid is riding his animal, Khalid goaded it, causing it to become startled or kick and kill someone. Liability is on Khalid, not Zaid. This is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. The rational proof for liability on the goader and not the rider: Because the rider and the mount are both pushed by the goader's push, so the animal's action is attributed to the goader as if he did it with his hand. And because the goader is transgressive in his causation, while the rider is not transgressive in his action, so his side is preponderant in imposing liability due to transgression. Even if he had stopped his animal on the road, the liability would be half on the rider and half on the goader, because he is also transgressive in stopping. Translation: Because the rider and the mount are both pushed by the goader's action, so the animal's action is attributed to the goader's side, as if the goader did this with his hand. And because the goader is transgressive in his causation, and the rider is not transgressive in his action, so due to transgression, the goader's side is preponderant in imposing compensation, up to the point that if his animal was standing on the road, the liability is half on the rider and half on the goader, because the rider is also transgressive in stopping. Explanation: Why is liability on the goader and not on the rider? Its rational proof is being explained. Its gist is that the goader has effectively pushed both the rider and the mount, so due to his action, the person died, so liability is obligatory upon him. Also, this is the reason: the goader is transgressive in his action, and the rider is not transgressive, so liability is obligatory on the transgressor. Ashraf al-Hidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah Volume 15, 339. Book of Blood Money. Yes, if the rider had stopped his horse on the road and someone goaded it, causing someone's death, then the rider is also transgressive because he stopped the horse on the road. So in this mentioned case, liability is half-half on both. If the animal kicks and kills the goader, his blood is wasted. And if the rider is also killed, blood money is on the goader's 'aqilah. He said: And if it kicks the goader, his blood is wasted because he is like one who commits aggression against himself. And if it throws the rider and kills him, his blood money is on the goader's 'aqilah because he is transgressive in his causation, and blood money is on the 'aqilah. Translation: The author said: And if the animal kicked the goader, his blood is wasted, because he is in the position of one who commits aggression against himself. And if the animal threw the rider and killed him, his blood money is on the goader's 'aqilah, because he is transgressive in his causation, and in this case blood money is on the 'aqilah. Explanation: If the goader is kicked and killed by the animal, his blood is wasted because he committed aggression against himself. And if the animal threw the rider and killed him, his blood money is obligatory on the goader's 'aqilah, because the goader is the cause of his death and transgression exists on the goader's side. And in such a case, blood money is on the 'aqilah, so blood money is obligatory on the 'aqilah. If due to the goader's provocation the animal jumps on someone and kills him, liability is on the goader. He said: And if due to his goading it jumps on a person or tramples him and kills him, that is upon the goader, not the rider, for the reason we have mentioned. And the one standing on his property and the one traveling are the same in this. Translation: The author said: And if due to the goader's goading the animal jumps on a person or tramples him and kills him, then this compensation is on the goader, not the rider, for the same reason we have stated. And the one standing in his property and the one traveling are equal. Explanation: If due to the goader's goading the animal jumped and killed someone or trampled him, then blood money is on the goader, not the rider. Its proof is the same as mentioned earlier. Then in the previous issue, a difference was stated between standing and moving, i.e., in the first case liability is on both, and in the second only on the goader. This difference applies when he stopped on the road. If he stopped in his own property, then the ruling for moving and standing is the same.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of al-Hidayah discusses the liability (daman) when an animal is goaded (nakhs) and causes death. The passage type is a ruling with proof, combining textual evidence from the Companions and rational reasoning. The core ruling: if a rider is on an animal in the road and another person goads it, causing the animal to kill someone (by kicking, striking with forelegs, shying, jumping, or trampling), the liability falls on the goader (al-nakhis), not the rider (al-rakib). This is attributed to 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. The rational proof is twofold: (1) the goader effectively pushes both rider and mount, so the animal's action is attributed to him as if he did it directly; (2) the goader is transgressive (muta'addi) in his causation, while the rider is not, so liability is on the transgressor. However, if the rider had wrongfully stopped the animal on the road, he becomes transgressive as well, and liability is shared half and half between rider and goader. If the animal kills the goader himself, his blood is wasted (hadar) because he aggressed against himself. If the animal kills the rider, the blood money (diyah) is on the goader's 'aqilah (tribal group responsible for paying diyah), because the goader's transgressive causation led to the rider's death. Finally, if the animal jumps or tramples due to goading, liability remains on the goader. A key point: the difference between a stationary and moving animal applies only when the animal is stopped on the public road; if the animal is on the owner's property, the ruling for stationary and moving is the same (both cases put liability on the goader alone). The commentary follows the author's order, first stating the ruling, then the textual proof (athar), then the rational proof, then exceptions and sub-cases.

Key Terms
الناخس (al-nākhiṣ): The person who goads or pricks the animal, causing it to move or react.
الراكب (al-rākib): The rider of the animal.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money, the financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs in cases of homicide or injury.
العاقلة (al-'āqilah): The tribal group or extended family responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a member who has committed unintentional homicide.
هدر (hadar): Blood that is wasted, meaning no blood money or retaliation is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Hidayah, if a person goads an animal and it kicks a passerby to death, upon whom is the liability?
A) The rider of the animal
B) The goader
C) The owner of the animal
D) Both the rider and the goader equally

Key Takeaway
The goader is liable for death caused by an animal he goaded, based on the practice of the Companions and the principle that the transgressor bears responsibility.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Refinements: Transgression, Shared Liability, and Special Cases
Rational proof and subcases expanding the ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of transgression (ta'addi) to determine whether the rider shares liability, and state the rulings when the goader or rider is killed by the animal.

Core Idea
The goader's transgression is key; if the rider also transgresses (e.g., stopping on the road), liability is shared — and a transgressor's own death is hadar.

The basic ruling that the goader is solely liable changes when the rider himself is transgressive. This lesson explores the rational basis, the exception for stopping on the road, and the subcases of the goader or rider being killed. We also see that the distinction between stationary and moving applies only on public roads.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational proof
The goader pushed both rider and mount, so the animal's act is attributed to him. The goader is transgressive, while the rider is not.
Transgression (ta'addi) means doing an act that is not legally permitted.
2
Identify the exception
If the rider stops the animal on the road (without necessity), he becomes transgressive, and liability is split half and half between rider and goader.
Stopping on the road is itself a transgression because it obstructs traffic.
3
Analyze the case of goader killed
If the animal kills the goader, his blood is hadar (wasted) because his own transgression caused it.
He is considered to have aggressed against himself.
4
Analyze the case of rider killed
If the animal kills the rider, the goader's 'āqilah pays diyah because the goader's transgressive causation led to the rider's death.
This is consistent with the goader's liability for third-party death.
5
Note the property distinction
The difference between stationary and moving applies only on public roads. On private property, the ruling is the same regardless of stationarity.
On private property, there is no transgression in standing; the right to stand is unrestricted.

ایک شخص اپنے چوپائے پر بیٹھا جارہاتھا کہ دوسرے نے آر مار دی جس سے وہ بد کا اور کسی کو
ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان آرمارنے والے پر ہے
قال و من سار على دابة فى الطريق فضربها رجل أو نخسها فنفحت رجلا أو ضربته بيدها أو نفرت قصدمته فقتلته كان ذالك على الناخس دون الراكب هو المروى عن عمر وابن مسعود رضى الله عنهما ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور جو چلا راستہ میں چوپائے پر پس ماردیا اس چوپائے کو کسی شخص نے یا اس کو آر مار دی پس چوپائے نے کسی شخص کو لات ماری یا اس کو اگلے پاؤں مارے یا بدک گیا پس اس کو ٹکر ماری پس اس کو قتل کر دیا تو اس کا ضمان آر مارنے والے پر ہوگا نہ کہ را کب پر عمر اور ابن مسعودؓ سے یہی مروی ہے۔
تشریح زید اپنے چوپائے پر بیٹھا جا رہا ہے، خالد نے اس کو آر مار دی جس سے وہ بد کا یا لات مار کر کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کا ضمان خالد پر ہوگا نہ کہ زید پر حضرت عمر اور ابن مسعودؓ سے یہی مروی ہے۔
ضمان مذکور ناخس پر ہے را کب پر نہیں اسکی عقلی دلیل
ولأن الراكب والمركب مدفوعان بدفع الناخس فأضيف فعل الدابة إليه كأنه فعله بيده ولأن الناخس متعد سبيبه والراكب في فعله غير متعد فيترجح جانبه فى التغريم للتعدى حتى لو كان واقفا دابته على الطريق يكون الضمان على الراكب والناخس نصفين لأنه متعد في الإيقاف أيضا ترجمہ اور اس لئے کہ راکب اور سواری کو دونوں کو دھکا دیا گیا ہے ناخس کے فعل سے تو چوپائے کا فعل ناخس کی جانب مضاف ہوگا گویا کہ ناخس نے یہ کام اپنے ہاتھ سے کیا ہے۔ اور اس لئے کہ ناخس اپنی تسبیب میں متعدی ہے اور را کب اپنے فعل میں متعدی نہیں ہے تو تعدی کی وجہ سے تاوان کے بارے میں ناخس کی جانب راجح ہوگی، یہاں تک کہ اس کا چوپایہ اگر راستہ میں کھڑا ہو تو ضمان را کب اور ناخس پر آدھا آدھا ہو گا اس لئے کہ کھڑا کرنے میں را کب بھی متعدی ہے۔
تشریح ضمان مذکور ناخس پر کیوں ہے اور راکب پر کیوں نہیں؟ اس کی عقلی دلیل بیان کی جارہی ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ناخس ہی نے گویا سوار اور سواری کو دھکا دیا ہے، لہذا اسی کے فعل کی وجہ سے مرنے والا مرا ہے لہذا ضمان اسی پر واجب ہوگا۔ نیز یہ بھی وجہ ہے کہ ناخس تو اپنے فعل میں متعدی ہے اور راکب متعدی نہیں ہے لہذا متعدی پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵
