كتاب الديات
ہاں اگر سوار نے اپنا گھوڑا راستہ میں کھڑا کر رکھا ہو اور کسی نے اس کو آر مار دی جس سے کسی کی ہلاکت ہو گئی تو اب سوار کی جانب سے بھی تعدی ہے کیونکہ اس نے گھوڑ از استہ میں کھڑا کیا ہے لہذا صورت مذکورہ میں ضمان ان دونوں پر آدھا آدھا آئے گا۔ ناخس کو چوپائے نے لات مار کر ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کا خون ہدر ہے اور اگر سوار بھی ہلاک ہو گیا تو دیت ناخس کی عاقلہ پر ہے
قال وإن نفحت الناخس كان دمه هدر لأنه بمنزلة الجانى على نفسه وإن ألقت الراكب فقتلته كان ديته على عاقلة النـاخــس لأنه متعد فى تسبيبه وفيه الدية على العاقلة ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر چوپائے نے ناخس کو لات مار دی تو اس کا خون رائیگاں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے نفس پر جنایت کرنے والے کے درجہ میں ہے اور اگر چوپائے نے سوار کو گرادیا پس اس کو قتل کر دیا تو اس کی دیت ناخس کے عاقلہ پر ہوگی اس لئے کہ وہ اپنی تسبیب میں متعدی ہے اور اس میں عاقلہ پر دیت ہے۔
تشریح اگر آر مارنے والے ہی کو چوپائے نے لات مار کر ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کا خون ہدر ہے کیونکہ اس نے اپنے نفس پر خود جنایت کی ہے اور اگر چوپائے نے سوار کو گرا کر ہلاک کم دیا تو اس کی دیت ناخس کے عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی کیونکہ ناخس اس کی موت کا سبب ہے اور ناخس کی جانب سے تعدی موجود ہے۔ اور ایسی صورت میں دیت عاقلہ پر ہوتی ہے لہذا عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگی ۔
ناخس کے چھیڑ نے سے چو پایہ کسی پر کودا اور اسے ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان ناخس پر ہوگا
قال ولو وثبت بنخسه على رجل أو وطئته فقتلته كان ذالك على الناخس دون الراكب لما بيناه والواقف في ملكه والذي يسير في ذالك سواء ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر ناخس کی آرسے چو پایہ کود پڑا کسی شخص پر یا اس کو روند دیا پس اس کوقتل کر دیا تو یہ تاوان ناخس پر ہو گا نہ کہ راکب پر اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ اور اپنی ملک میں کھڑا ہونے والا اور جو اپنی ملک میں چلے
برابر ہے۔
تشریح اگر ناخس کی آر کی وجہ سے چوپائے نے کود کر کسی کو قتل کر دیا ہو یا بروند کر تو اس کی دیت ناخس پر ہوگی را کب پر نہیں۔ اس کی دلیل وہی ہے جو ابھی مذکور ہوئی۔ پھر ماقبل والے مسئلہ میں واقف اور چلنے والے میں فرق بیان کیا گیا ہے یعنی اول صورت میں ضمان دونوں پر ہے اور ثانی میں فقط ناخس پر ہے۔ یہ فرق جب ہے جب کہ اس نے راستہ میں کھڑا کیا ہو اور اگر اپنی ملک میں کھڑا کیا ہو تو پھر چلنے اور کھڑے ہونے کا حکم ایک ہے۔ کا
Book of Blood Money. 248. Ashraf al-Bidaya Sharh Urdu Hidaya Volume 15. ... (previous passage on eye injury). A person was sitting on his animal, another goaded it, causing it to become startled and kill someone. The liability is on the goader. He said: Whoever travels on an animal in the road, and a man strikes it or goads it, and it kicks a person, or strikes him with its forelegs, or shies and collides with him and kills him, that is upon the goader, not the rider. This is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with them. Translation: The author said: And whoever travels on an animal in the road, then a person struck that animal or goaded it, and the animal kicked a person, or struck him with its forelegs, or shied and collided with him and killed him, then liability is on the goader, not the rider. This is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. Explanation: Zaid is riding his animal, Khalid goaded it, causing it to become startled or kick and kill someone. Liability is on Khalid, not Zaid. This is narrated from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. The rational proof for liability on the goader and not the rider: Because the rider and the mount are both pushed by the goader's push, so the animal's action is attributed to the goader as if he did it with his hand. And because the goader is transgressive in his causation, while the rider is not transgressive in his action, so his side is preponderant in imposing liability due to transgression. Even if he had stopped his animal on the road, the liability would be half on the rider and half on the goader, because he is also transgressive in stopping. Translation: Because the rider and the mount are both pushed by the goader's action, so the animal's action is attributed to the goader's side, as if the goader did this with his hand. And because the goader is transgressive in his causation, and the rider is not transgressive in his action, so due to transgression, the goader's side is preponderant in imposing compensation, up to the point that if his animal was standing on the road, the liability is half on the rider and half on the goader, because the rider is also transgressive in stopping. Explanation: Why is liability on the goader and not on the rider? Its rational proof is being explained. Its gist is that the goader has effectively pushed both the rider and the mount, so due to his action, the person died, so liability is obligatory upon him. Also, this is the reason: the goader is transgressive in his action, and the rider is not transgressive, so liability is obligatory on the transgressor. Ashraf al-Hidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah Volume 15, 339. Book of Blood Money. Yes, if the rider had stopped his horse on the road and someone goaded it, causing someone's death, then the rider is also transgressive because he stopped the horse on the road. So in this mentioned case, liability is half-half on both. If the animal kicks and kills the goader, his blood is wasted. And if the rider is also killed, blood money is on the goader's 'aqilah. He said: And if it kicks the goader, his blood is wasted because he is like one who commits aggression against himself. And if it throws the rider and kills him, his blood money is on the goader's 'aqilah because he is transgressive in his causation, and blood money is on the 'aqilah. Translation: The author said: And if the animal kicked the goader, his blood is wasted, because he is in the position of one who commits aggression against himself. And if the animal threw the rider and killed him, his blood money is on the goader's 'aqilah, because he is transgressive in his causation, and in this case blood money is on the 'aqilah. Explanation: If the goader is kicked and killed by the animal, his blood is wasted because he committed aggression against himself. And if the animal threw the rider and killed him, his blood money is obligatory on the goader's 'aqilah, because the goader is the cause of his death and transgression exists on the goader's side. And in such a case, blood money is on the 'aqilah, so blood money is obligatory on the 'aqilah. If due to the goader's provocation the animal jumps on someone and kills him, liability is on the goader. He said: And if due to his goading it jumps on a person or tramples him and kills him, that is upon the goader, not the rider, for the reason we have mentioned. And the one standing on his property and the one traveling are the same in this. Translation: The author said: And if due to the goader's goading the animal jumps on a person or tramples him and kills him, then this compensation is on the goader, not the rider, for the same reason we have stated. And the one standing in his property and the one traveling are equal. Explanation: If due to the goader's goading the animal jumped and killed someone or trampled him, then blood money is on the goader, not the rider. Its proof is the same as mentioned earlier. Then in the previous issue, a difference was stated between standing and moving, i.e., in the first case liability is on both, and in the second only on the goader. This difference applies when he stopped on the road. If he stopped in his own property, then the ruling for moving and standing is the same.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of al-Hidayah discusses the liability (daman) when an animal is goaded (nakhs) and causes death. The passage type is a ruling with proof, combining textual evidence from the Companions and rational reasoning. The core ruling: if a rider is on an animal in the road and another person goads it, causing the animal to kill someone (by kicking, striking with forelegs, shying, jumping, or trampling), the liability falls on the goader (al-nakhis), not the rider (al-rakib). This is attributed to 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. The rational proof is twofold: (1) the goader effectively pushes both rider and mount, so the animal's action is attributed to him as if he did it directly; (2) the goader is transgressive (muta'addi) in his causation, while the rider is not, so liability is on the transgressor. However, if the rider had wrongfully stopped the animal on the road, he becomes transgressive as well, and liability is shared half and half between rider and goader. If the animal kills the goader himself, his blood is wasted (hadar) because he aggressed against himself. If the animal kills the rider, the blood money (diyah) is on the goader's 'aqilah (tribal group responsible for paying diyah), because the goader's transgressive causation led to the rider's death. Finally, if the animal jumps or tramples due to goading, liability remains on the goader. A key point: the difference between a stationary and moving animal applies only when the animal is stopped on the public road; if the animal is on the owner's property, the ruling for stationary and moving is the same (both cases put liability on the goader alone). The commentary follows the author's order, first stating the ruling, then the textual proof (athar), then the rational proof, then exceptions and sub-cases.

Key Terms
الناخس (al-nākhiṣ): The person who goads or pricks the animal, causing it to move or react.
الراكب (al-rākib): The rider of the animal.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money, the financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs in cases of homicide or injury.
العاقلة (al-'āqilah): The tribal group or extended family responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a member who has committed unintentional homicide.
هدر (hadar): Blood that is wasted, meaning no blood money or retaliation is due.
تعدي (ta'addī): Transgression, exceeding the lawful bounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a rider stops his horse on a public road and a goader strikes it, causing the horse to kill a pedestrian, how is liability distributed?
A) The goader is fully liable
B) The rider is fully liable
C) The rider and goader each pay half
D) The animal's owner pays

Key Takeaway
Transgression is the key variable: the goader's transgression makes him primarily liable, but the rider's transgression (stopping on road) splits liability; the goader's own death from the animal is hadar, while the rider's death obligates the goader's 'āqilah.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Liability When a Provoked Animal Kills the Provoker or Rider
States rulings on when provoked animal kills provoker or rider.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a provoked animal kills the provoker or the rider, and explain the reasoning based on causation and transgression.

Core Idea
The provoker's own death is hadar (forfeit), but if the rider dies, the provoker's 'aqila must pay diya.

This lesson examines two scenarios involving a provoked animal: when it kills the person who provoked it (al-nakhis), and when it kills the rider. The Hanafi school distinguishes between them based on who is considered the cause of death and whether transgression (ta'addi) is present. Understanding these rulings clarifies the principles of indirect causation and the role of the 'aqila in blood money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two distinct cases: (1) the animal kicks and kills the provoker, and (2) the animal throws and kills the rider. Note the different rulings for each.
The provoker is called al-nakhis, the rider is al-rakib.
2
State the ruling for the provoker's death
If the animal kills the provoker, his blood is hadar (forfeit) — no diya or qisas is due. The reason given is that he is like one who commits aggression against himself (ka-annahu jani 'ala nafsihi).
This is because his own act of provocation caused the animal to react.
3
State the ruling for the rider's death
If the animal kills the rider, the full diya (blood money) is due on the 'aqila (male paternal relatives) of the provoker. The reason is that the provoker's act of prodding was a transgression (ta'addi) that caused the rider's death.
The diya is on the 'aqila, not on the provoker personally, following Hanafi principles for indirect killing.
4
Explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The provoker's prodding is a direct cause (sabab) of the animal's reaction. If he himself dies, his own transgression is the cause — thus no compensation. If the rider dies, the provoker's transgression is the cause of an innocent's death, so liability falls on his 'aqila.
This distinction is based on the presence of transgression and who is the ultimate victim.

Source Text

ہاں اگر سوار نے اپنا گھوڑا راستہ میں کھڑا کر رکھا ہو اور کسی نے اس کو آر مار دی جس سے کسی کی ہلاکت ہو گئی تو اب سوار کی جانب سے بھی تعدی ہے کیونکہ اس نے گھوڑا راستہ میں کھڑا کیا ہے لہذا صورت مذکورہ میں ضمان ان دونوں پر آدھا آدھا آئے گا۔ ناخس کو چوپائے نے لات مار کر ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کا خون ہدر ہے اور اگر سوار بھی ہلاک ہو گیا تو دیت ناخس کی عاقلہ پر ہے
قال وإن نفحت الناخس كان دمه هدر لأنه بمنزلة الجانى على نفسه وإن ألقت الراكب فقتلته كان ديته على عاقلة الناخس لأنه متعد فى تسبيبه وفيه الدية على العاقلة
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر چوپائے نے ناخس کو لات مار دی تو اس کا خون رائیگاں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے نفس پر جنایت کرنے والے کے درجہ میں ہے اور اگر چوپائے نے سوار کو گرادیا پس اس کو قتل کر دیا تو اس کی دیت ناخس کے عاقلہ پر ہوگی اس لئے کہ وہ اپنی تسبیب میں متعدی ہے اور اس میں عاقلہ پر دیت ہے۔
تشریح اگر آر مارنے والے ہی کو چوپائے نے لات مار کر ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کا خون ہدر ہے کیونکہ اس نے اپنے نفس پر خود جنایت کی ہے اور اگر چوپائے نے سوار کو گرا کر ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کی دیت ناخس کے عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی کیونکہ ناخس اس کی موت کا سبب ہے اور ناخس کی جانب سے تعدی موجود ہے۔ اور ایسی صورت میں دیت عاقلہ پر ہوتی ہے لہذا عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگی ۔
Yes, if the rider has stationed his horse on the road, and someone provoked it, causing someone's death, then there is transgression from the rider as well because he stationed the horse on the road. Therefore, in the mentioned situation, the liability falls half on both. If the animal kicks the provoker and kills him, his blood is forfeit. And if the rider is also killed, then the blood money is on the 'aqila of the provoker. The author said: 'And if the animal kicks the provoker, his blood is forfeit because he is like one who commits aggression against himself. And if it throws the rider and kills him, his blood money is on the 'aqila of the provoker because he is transgressing in his causation, and in this case the blood money is on the 'aqila.' Translation: The author said: If the animal kicks the provoker, his blood is wasted, because he is like one who commits a crime against himself. And if the animal throws the rider and kills him, then his blood money is on the 'aqila of the provoker, because he is transgressing in his causation, and in this case the blood money is on the 'aqila. Explanation: If the animal kicks and kills the one who provoked it, his blood is forfeit because he himself committed aggression against himself. And if the animal throws and kills the rider, then his blood money is obligatory on the 'aqila of the provoker, because the provoker is the cause of his death and there is transgression on the part of the provoker. And in such a case, the blood money is on the 'aqila; therefore, the blood money is obligatory on the 'aqila.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and reasoning. It addresses two scenarios involving a provoked animal. First, if the animal kicks and kills the person who provoked it (al-nakhis), his blood is forfeit (hadar) because he is considered to have committed aggression against himself by his own act of provocation. Second, if the animal throws off the rider and kills him, the blood money (diya) is due on the 'aqila (the male paternal relatives) of the provoker, because the provoker's act of prodding was a transgression (ta'addi) that caused the rider's death, and in such cases of indirect causation with transgression, the diya is on the 'aqila. The text explicitly states the reasoning: the provoker is like one who commits a crime against himself in the first case, and in the second, his causation is transgressive, so the ’aqila bears the diya. The commentary explains that the provocation is the cause (sabab) and since transgression exists, the liability transfers to the 'aqila, which is the general principle in Hanafi law for such cases.

Key Terms
ناخس (al-nākhis): The person who prods or provokes an animal, causing it to move or react.
عاقلة (al-ʿāqila): The male paternal relatives of the killer who are responsible for paying diya in cases of accidental or semi-intentional killing.
هدر (hadar): Blood that is forfeit, meaning no diya or qisas is due.

D) Qisas is due on the horse.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a provoked animal killing the provoker is hadar, but killing the rider requires diya on the provoker's 'aqila due to transgressive causation.

Liability of the Provoker When a Provoked Animal Kills a Third Party
Ruling on liability when a provoked animal kills a third party, with distinction based on rider's location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on liability when a provoked animal kills a third person and identify the condition under which the rider shares liability.

Core Idea
The provoker is fully liable for killing caused by his provocation; the rider is only liable if he himself committed transgression.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario: an animal provoked by a third party (nakhis) jumps on or tramples a bystander. The immediate ruling is clear—the nakhis pays the diya, not the rider. However, the text hints at a critical distinction based on the rider's location, which can shift liability. We will unpack this ruling and its underlying principle of transgression (ta'addi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The animal kills a third person due to provocation. The liability for diya is on the nakhis, not the rider.
This is the basic ruling stated in the passage.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the principle that liability follows transgression (ta'addi). The nakhis committed ta'addi by provoking; the rider did not if he was not transgressing.
The text references an earlier explanation; we infer the general principle.
3
Trace the distinction based on rider's location
If the rider stood in the public road, that itself is a transgression, making him share liability. If he stood in his own property, there is no transgression from him.
The commentary clarifies this distinction.
4
Apply the rule to different scenarios
In all cases where the rider is not transgressive (e.g., in his own property or walking without wrongdoing), the nakhis alone is liable. If the rider is transgressive, both share liability equally.
This completes the legal analysis.

Source Text

Due to the nakhis's provocation, if the animal jumps on someone and kills him, then the liability is on the nakhis. And the one standing in his property and the one moving are equal in this.' Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, addressing a scenario where a provoked animal jumps on or tramples a third person (not the rider or the provoker). The text establishes that the liability (damān) is entirely on the provoker (nakhis), not on the rider. The proof is the same as earlier: the provoker's act was transgressive. The passage then makes a critical distinction based on the rider's location. In a previous ruling, if the rider stood his horse in the public road and the provoker acted, both were liable half-half. But here, if the rider is merely present (not stationary in the road as a transgressor), the entire liability falls on the provoker. However, if the rider stood in his own property (i.e., not in the public road), then there is no transgression from the rider, and the liability remains solely on the provoker regardless of whether the rider is standing or moving. The commentary explains that the distinction of location (public road vs. private property) is crucial: standing in the road is itself a transgression, leading to shared liability, whereas standing in one's own property is not transgressive, so the rider is not liable.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to pay compensation, here specifically the blood money (diya) for the killing.
تعدی (taʿaddī): Transgression, i.e., an act that goes beyond what is permissible, making one liable for resulting harm.

Imam Abu Yusuf's View on Half-Half Liability
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shared liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Yusuf's position on half-half liability in the case of a rider killed by a provoked animal, and explain the effect of the rider's permission on the provoker's liability.

Core Idea
When both rider's weight and animal's trampling cause death, liability is shared half-half; permission transfers the action to the rider.

In this passage, we encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for diya when a rider is killed after provoking an animal. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees with the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that the entire diya falls on the provoker. Instead, he argues for a half-half split between the provoker and the rider (or his estate). He also discusses a scenario where the provoker acted with the rider's permission, which shifts liability entirely to the rider. This lesson will guide you through his reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
First, recall the majority Hanafi ruling: when a rider is killed due to the provocation of an animal, the entire diya is on the 'aqila of the provoker. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
This base ruling is the starting point for Abu Yusuf's dissent.
2
State Abu Yusuf's objection and his half-half division
Abu Yusuf argues that the death results from both the rider's weight (thiqal al-rakib) and the animal's trampling (wat' al-dabba). Since both are causes, liability should be shared equally between the provoker and the rider.
The rider's share falls on his estate or heirs, as he is the victim.
3
Explain the causal reasoning (ta'lil)
The trampling is attributed to the provoker (nakhis) because he caused it. The weight is directly from the rider. Therefore, each is responsible for his contribution to the death.
This is a ta'lil (causal reasoning) based on direct and indirect causation.
4
Analyze the permission scenario and transfer of action
If the provoker acted with the rider's permission, his action is transferred to the rider, as if the rider did it himself. Consequently, if the animal kicks someone, the provoker is not liable; the rider is treated as the doer.
This is similar to agency: the rider's command (amr) makes him the principal.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees with the earlier ruling (attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that in the case where the rider is killed, the entire diya is on the provoker's 'aqila. Abu Yusuf argues that the rider's weight also contributes to the death, so the liability should be shared half between the provoker and the rider (as heirs? Actually the rider is dead, so his share goes to his heirs? Wait: the context is when the rider is killed. The liability is on the provoker's 'aqila for half and on the rider's own 'aqila for the other half? The text says 'ala al-nakhis wa al-rakib nisfayn. But the rider is dead, so the payment comes from his estate? Actually in Hanafi law, if the rider is also at fault, his 'aqila might also be liable. The commentary clarifies: the death was caused by both the rider's weight and the animal's trampling, so both are causes. Therefore, the provoker is liable for half (his share) and the rider's side also bears half (presumably from his heirs or 'aqila). The passage then adds a second point: if the nakhis acted with the rider's permission, then the nakhis's action is attributed to the rider (since he commanded it), so if the animal kicks someone, the nakhis is not liable for the kick; the rider is treated as the doer. However, if the rider had not commanded and the horse kicks someone while walking, the rider is not liable. This additional ruling shows the transfer of liability through command (amr).

ناخس (nākhis): The person who provokes the animal (by poking or urging).

Immediate Trampling After Permitted Provocation: Shared Diya Liability
Ruling when animal tramples immediately after permitted provocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diya when an animal tramples someone immediately after a permitted provocation, and explain why liability is shared equally between the rider and the provoker.

Core Idea
Prompt provocation + rider's permission = shared blood money half and half.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in tort liability (diyat). When a rider permits someone to prod his animal, and the animal immediately tramples a bystander, who bears the blood money? The Hanafi school provides a precise answer: the rider and the provoker share the diya equally. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, focusing on the condition of immediacy (fawr) and the attribution of the animal's movement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and the action
The passage involves three parties: the rider (who gives permission), the nakhis (who prods the animal), and the victim (who is trampled). The action is a permitted provocation that immediately leads to trampling.
Note the permission: without it, liability would likely fall solely on the provoker.
2
Understand the condition 'fawr' (immediacy)
The ruling explicitly requires that the trampling occur 'fi fawriha alladhi nakhasha' — immediately as a result of the prod. This means no intervening event; the animal's reaction is direct and unbroken.
If the trampling is not immediate, the attribution may shift entirely to the provoker.
3
Analyze the attribution of the animal's movement
The text states that the animal's walking (sir) is 'attributed to both' (mudaf ilayhima). Because the rider permitted the driving (sawq), he shares responsibility for the movement.
Hanafi principle: causation (ta'ddi) is determined by contribution to the direct cause.
4
Distinguish between permission to drive (sawq) and permission to destroy (itlaf)
The rider's permission covers the act of driving, but not the act of destruction. Therefore, the destructive aspect (itlaf) is confined to the provoker. Yet because the movement itself is a joint creation, both bear liability for the consequence.
This is a key nuance: permission is limited in scope.
5
Conclude the liability distribution
Since both contributed to the walking that led to the trampling, and the immediacy condition is satisfied, the diya is split half-half between the rider and the provoker.
The ruling is half on both, not full on one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ناخس نے راکب کی اجازت سے آر ماری چوپایہ بدکا اور کسی کو روند ڈالا تو دیت دونوں پر ہے
قال ولو وطئت رجلا في سيرها و قد نخسها الناخس باذن الراكب فالدية عليهما نصفين جميعًا اذا كانت في فورها الذي نخسها لان سيرها فى تلك الحالة مضاف اليهما والاذن يتناول فعله السوق ولا يتناوله من حيث انه اتلاف فمن هذا الوجه يقتصر عليه
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر چوپائے نے اپنے چلنے میں کسی شخص کو روند دیا حالانکہ اس کو ناخس نے راکب کی اجازت سے آر لگائی تھی تو ان دونوں پر دیت آدھی آدھی ہو گی جب کہ روندنا فوراً اس شخص کی وجہ سے ہو جو آر اس نے لگائی ہے اس لئے کہ اس حالت میں چوپائے کی چال ان دونوں کی جانب مضاف ہوگی اور اجازت شامل ہے ناخس کے فعل سوق کو اور اس کے فعل کو شامل نہیں ہے اتلاف کی حیثیت سے پس اس وجہ سے اختلاف ناخس پر منحصر ہے۔
The nakhis struck with the rider's permission; the animal shied and trampled someone; so the blood money is on both. The author said: 'And if it tramples a man while walking, and the nakhis had provoked it with the rider's permission, then the blood money is half on both of them, provided that it is immediately as a result of the provocation, because the animal's walking in that state is attributed to both, and the permission includes his act of driving, but does not include it from the aspect of destruction, so from this aspect it is confined to the nakhis.' Translation: The author said: And if the animal while walking tramples a person, and the nakhis had given it a prod with the rider's permission, then the blood money is half on both of them, when the trampling occurs immediately due to the prod he gave, because in that state the animal's walking is attributed to both, and the permission includes the nakhis's act of driving, but does not include it from the aspect of destruction, so due to this aspect the difference (of liability) depends on the nakhis.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning, addressing a specific scenario: the provoker (nakhis) acts with the rider's permission, and the animal immediately tramples a third person. The ruling is that the blood money (diya) is split half-half between the provoker and the rider. The reason given is that the animal's movement at that moment is attributed to both the rider (who gave permission) and the provoker (who did the actual prodding). However, the permission only covers the act of driving (sawq), not the act of destruction (itlaf). Therefore, while both are liable for the walking, the destruction aspect is confined to the provoker, but since both contributed to the walking that led to the destruction, they share liability. The commentary explains that if the trampling is immediate (fawran) upon the provocation, then the movement is connected to both. If it is not immediate, then perhaps liability would only be on the provoker? The text implies that the immediacy is crucial: 'idha kanat fi fawriha alladhi nakhasha' meaning 'when it is in the immediate result of his provocation.' This limits the shared liability to cases where the trampling is a direct, unbroken consequence.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): Immediately, without interruption; the condition that the trampling occurs as a direct result of the provocation.
سوق (sawq): Driving or urging the animal, which is considered permissible if it does not cause destruction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only the rider.
B) Only the provoker (nakhis).
C) Half on the rider and half on the provoker.
D) No one, because it was an accident.

Key Takeaway
When a permitted provocation immediately results in trampling, the blood money is divided equally between the rider and the provoker due to their joint causal contribution.

Abu Yusuf's View on Shared Liability of Goader and Rider
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shared liability of goader and rider

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Yusuf's position on apportionment of diyat when a goader and rider cause death, and explain how permission transfers liability to the rider.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf splits diyat half-half; with rider's permission, the goader's act transfers.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when an animal kills due to the combined actions of a goader (nakhis) and a rider (rakib). The earlier established view (likely of Imam Abu Hanifa) places full liability on the goader. However, Imam Abu Yusuf offers a nuanced alternative, splitting the blood money equally and introducing the effect of the rider's permission. By the end, you will be able to articulate both positions and understand the reasoning behind the transfer of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage concerns liability (daman) for diyat when an animal causes death due to the goader's provocation and the rider's weight. Determine what the base ruling is and where Abu Yusuf differs.
Focus on the causal factors: rider's weight (thiqal) and animal's trampling (wat' al-dabbah).
2
State Abu Yusuf's ruling without permission
Abu Yusuf holds that liability is half on the goader and half on the rider. Trace his reasoning: death results from both causes, so both are liable.
Contrast with the implied view of Abu Hanifa that liability is full on the goader.
3
Analyse the permission scenario
When the goader acts with the rider's permission, Abu Yusuf considers the act as if performed by the rider himself. The goader's act transfers to the rider because goading is analogous to driving (saq)—an act the rider owns.
Why does permission transfer liability? The rider has the right to drive, so his command is valid.
4
Distinguish trampling from kicking
In the permission scenario, if the animal kicks (nafhat), there is no liability on the goader or the rider because the kick is an unexpected animal action. However, if the animal tramples due to the goading, the liability remains on both (since permission transfers only the goading act, not the animal's autonomous act).
The commentary clarifies: goading transferred, but the kick is not caused by goading, so no liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in a case without permission, Abu Hanifa's view imposes full diyat on the goader; Abu Yusuf splits it. With permission, Abu Yusuf transfers liability entirely to the rider for trampling, while Abu Hanifa might still hold the goader.
The mufta bihi is not specified in the passage; both views are considered valid within the school.

و عن أبي يوسف أنه يجب الضمان على الناخس والراكب نصفين لأن التلف حصل بثقل الراكب ووطىء الدانة والثانى مضاف إلى الناخس فيجب الضمان عليهما وإن نخسها بإذن الراكب كان ذالك بمنزلة فعل الراكب لو نخسها ولا ضمان عليه فى نفحتها لأنه أمره بما يملكه إذ النخس فى معنى السوق فصح أمره به وانتقل إليه لمعنى الأمر

تشریح صورت مذکورہ میں جہاں پور اضمان ناخس پر واجب کیا گیا ہے امام ابو یوسف نے منمان را کب اور ناخس دونوں پر آدھا آدھا کیا ہے کیونکہ مقتول کا مرنا سوار کے بوجھ اور چوپائے کے روندنے کی وجہ سے ہے۔ اور چوپائے کا روند نا ناخس کی جانب مضاف ہوگا۔ الہذا ضمان دونوں پر واجب ہوگا۔ اور اگر ناخس نے راکب کی اجازت سے مارا ہو تو اب ناخس کا فعل راکب کی جانب منتقل ہو جائے گا گویا کہ راکب نے یہ کام خود کیا ہے۔ اگر چلتا ہوا گھوڑا کسی کو پیچھے سے لات مار دے تو را کب پر ضمان نہیں آتا ( کما مر ) اور اگر راکب نے حق کو مارنے کا حکم کیا جس کی وجہ سے گھوڑے نے کسی کو لات ماردی تو چونکہ ناخس کا فعل را کب کی جانب منتقل ہو جائے گا اور راکب کو اس کی اجازت ہے کہ وہ خوداس کام کو کر سکتا ہے، کیونکہ یہ ہانکنے کے درجہ میں ہے، اس لئے اب لات کا ضمان نہ ناخس پر ہوگا اور نہ راکب پر ۔ ناخس نے راکب کی اجازت سے آر ماری چو پا یہ بد کا اور کسی کو روند ڈالا تو دیت دونوں پر ہے
And from Abu Yusuf: liability is required on the goader and the rider in halves, because the destruction occurred by the weight of the rider and the trampling of the animal, and the second (trampling) is attributed to the goader, so liability is upon both. And if he goaded it with the rider's permission, that is like the rider's own act if he had goaded it, and there is no liability upon him for its kick, because he ordered him with something he owns, since goading is in the meaning of driving, so his order is valid and the act transfers to him due to the meaning of the order. Translation: And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that indemnity is half and half on the goader and the rider, because the destruction occurred from the rider's weight and the animal's trampling, and the second (animal's trampling) is attributed to the goader, so liability will be on both. And if he goaded with the rider's permission, then this is like the rider's act if he himself had goaded, and there is no liability on the goader for the animal's kick, because the rider ordered him with something he owns, because goading is in the meaning of driving, so the rider's order is valid and the act transfers to the rider due to the order. Explanation: In the mentioned case where full liability was imposed on the goader, Imam Abu Yusuf made it half and half on both rider and goader, because the victim's death is due to the rider's weight and the animal's trampling. And the animal's trampling is attributed to the goader. Therefore liability is on both. And if the goader acted with the rider's permission, then the goader's act transfers to the rider, as if the rider did it himself. If a walking horse kicks someone from behind, there is no liability on the rider (as previously). And if the rider ordered to strike the animal causing it to kick someone, since the goader's act transfers to the rider and the rider has permission to do that act himself (since it is in the meaning of driving), therefore there is no liability on the goader nor on the rider for the kick. If the goader struck with the rider's permission and the animal trampled someone, then the blood money is on both.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when an animal causes death due to the combined actions of a rider and a goader. The author first quotes Abu Yusuf's position, which differs from the earlier stated view (likely of Abu Hanifa) that full liability falls on the goader. Abu Yusuf argues that both the rider's weight and the goader's provocation contribute to the death, so liability is shared equally. He then addresses a scenario where the goader acts with the rider's permission: in such a case, the act of goading is transferred to the rider, making the rider solely responsible. Consequently, if the animal kicks (without trampling), the goader bears no liability because the rider authorized an act he could legally perform himself, and that act is akin to driving. The commentary elaborates, emphasizing that the rider's ownership of the right to drive the animal validates his command, and the transference of action via command is a key legal principle. The passage ends with a summary that when goading with permission leads to trampling, blood money is due on both.

Key Terms
ناخس (nakhis): The person who goads or pricks an animal to make it move.
راكب (rakib): The rider of the animal.
ضمان (daman): Financial liability, here specifically the obligation to pay diyah (blood money).
إذن (idhn): Permission given by the rider to the goader to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf in this passage, what is the liability distribution when a goader without permission causes a rider's animal to trample someone to death?
A) Full liability on the goader only
B) Full liability on the rider only
C) Half on the goader and half on the rider
D) No liability on either

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf apportions diyat half-half between goader and rider, and the rider's permission transfers the goading act to the rider, affecting liability for subsequent harm.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Shared Liability: Goading and Riding as Co-Causes in Trampling
Ruling with causal analysis and analogy for shared diyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that the rider and goader share diyah equally when an animal tramples immediately after goading with permission, and apply the qiyas of wounding and well-digging to justify shared liability.

Core Idea
When two separate causes combine to produce death, liability is shared — riding and goading are co-causes, not cause and condition.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced Hanafi ruling on shared liability for diyah. When an animal tramples a person immediately after being goaded with the rider's permission, both the rider and the goader must pay half the blood money. The key lies in causal attribution: the animal's movement at that moment is attributed to both parties. We will work through the ruling, the condition of immediacy, a likely objection, and the supporting analogy from wounding and well-digging.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Condition
State the Hanafi position: diyah is on both rider and goader equally, but only if trampling occurs immediately after goading. If delayed, only the rider is liable.
Immediacy is crucial; the goader's act must still be the effective cause.
2
Analyze the Attribution of Movement
Understand why the animal's movement is attributed to both: riding controls the animal, while goading provokes immediate motion. The permission covers driving, not causing loss, so the goader still bears responsibility.
The permission is for the act of driving (saq), not for destruction (itlaf).
3
Consider the Objection
Anticipate the objection: riding is the direct cause of trampling, and goading is merely a condition; liability should fall solely on the rider.
This objection confuses a condition for a cause.
4
Respond with Causal Distinction
The Hanafi response: goading is not a condition for the cause (riding); it is a condition or cause for movement, which itself is the cause of trampling. Thus two causes combine, and both are liable.
A condition precedes the cause; here goading occurs after riding? The text clarifies that goading is not a condition for riding's causality.
5
Apply the Analogy (Qiyas)
Draw the analogy: wounding a person who then falls into a well dug by another on a public road. Both the wounder and the digger share liability because their actions are separate causes contributing to death. Similarly, riding and goading are separate causes.
The common illa: two independent actions each causally contribute to the death.

He said: And if it (the animal) trampled a man while walking, and the goader had goaded it with the rider's permission, then the blood money is on both halves equally, when it (trampling) was in the immediate aftermath of the goading, because the animal's movement in that state is attributed to both, and the permission includes his act of driving, not his act of causing destruction, so from this aspect it is limited to him. Translation: The author said: And if the animal trampled a person while walking, and the goader had goaded it with the rider's permission, then the blood money is half and half on both, provided the trampling is immediate due to the goad, because the animal's movement in that state is attributed to both, and the permission includes the goader's act of driving, not his act of causing destruction, so from this aspect the goader is solely liable? [Note: the Arabic says 'yaktasiru 'alayhi' – it is confined to him, but the context suggests both are liable; the translation is ambiguous in the object.] Explanation: If the rider authorized Khalid to strike, so he struck, and the animal trampled someone, then both rider and goader are liable for the blood money. But it is necessary that the animal tramples at the moment of the strike; otherwise, if it tramples later, the goader's effect ends and he is not liable. In the immediate case, both are guarantors because the animal's movement is attributed to both. Question: When the goader struck on the rider's order, why is there liability on the goader? Answer: Zaid authorized Khalid to drive, not to kill. The demand of this aspect is that liability would be only on the goader, but we made it obligatory on both because of the act of goading. Answer to an anticipated objection: And riding, even if it is the cause of trampling, the goading is not a condition for this cause; rather it is a condition or cause for movement, and movement is the cause for trampling, and because of this the owner of the cause (rider) does not take precedence. Translation: And riding, although it is the cause of trampling, goading is not a condition for this cause; rather it is a condition or cause for movement, and movement is the cause for trampling, and because of this the owner of the cause (rider) is not preponderant. Explanation: This is an answer to an anticipated objection. The objection is: The rider's act is the cause of trampling, as if he himself trampled with his foot, and the goader's act is a condition. When the cause is effective, the ruling is attributed to the cause, not the condition, so liability should be on the rider. Answer: A condition occurs before the cause, and here the goading occurred after mounting? Actually the text says: 'the condition occurs first, and here the goading is after the riding' – this indicates that the goading is not a condition for the riding's causality; rather it is a condition or cause for movement, which is the cause of trampling. Thus two causes combine: goading and riding. Therefore liability is on both. So the rider has no precedence over the goader, and they are equal in liability. Analogy of the mentioned issue: Like someone who wounds a person, and the wounded falls into a well dug by another on the public road, and dies; then blood money is on both, because digging is a condition for another cause (falling) separate from the cause of the wound. Similarly here. Translation: Like someone who wounds a person, and the wounded falls into a well dug by others on the public road, and dies, then blood money is on both, because digging is a condition for the other cause (falling) not for the cause of wounding. Similarly here. Explanation: Zaid wounded Amr, and due to agitation he fell into a well dug by Khalid on the public road, and Amr died.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a specific scenario: when an animal tramples a person immediately after being goaded with the rider's permission. The author states that both the rider and the goader share diyah equally. The reasoning is that the animal's movement at that moment is attributed to both: the rider controls the animal through riding, and the goader provokes immediate movement. The permission only covers the act of driving (saq), not the act of causing loss (itlaf); thus from the itlaf perspective, the goader alone would be liable, but because the permission was for driving, the goader still bears responsibility. The commentary adds that immediacy is crucial: if the trampling occurs later, the goader's act is no longer the effective cause. The passage then answers an anticipated objection: why is the goader liable when he acted on orders? The answer distinguishes between authorization for driving (which is permissible) and authorization for killing (which is not). The objection argues that riding is the direct cause of trampling, and the goading is merely a condition; but the answer shows that the goading is a condition for movement, which is an intermediate cause. Since both actions contribute to the death, both are liable. Finally, an analogy is given: a person who wounds another and the wounded falls into a well dug by someone else on the public road – both are liable for the death because the wound and the fall combine as independent causal factors. This reinforces the principle that multiple proximate causes can share liability.

Key Terms
علة (illa): The effective cause that directly produces a legal effect, here used for riding (cause of trampling) and movement (cause of trampling).
شرط (shart): A condition that is necessary for the effect but not the direct cause; here goading is said to be a condition for movement.
سوق (saq): Driving or urging the animal forward, which is considered a permissible act for the rider to delegate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for making both the rider and the goader liable for trampling that occurs immediately after goading with permission?
A) The goader's act is the sole cause of the trampling
B) The rider's act alone is the cause, but the goader is also liable due to his permission
C) The animal's movement at that moment is attributed to both the rider's riding and the goader's goading
D) The rider is not liable because he gave permission

Key Takeaway
When two separate causes combine to produce death, Hanafi law imposes shared diyah on both actors, as illustrated by the analogy of wounding and well-digging.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Liability of Rider and Goader When Animal Tramples
Ruling on liability when rider authorizes goading and animal tramples someone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when two independent causes (illa) lead to death, both actors are jointly liable for diyat, and you will be able to distinguish between a cause and a condition in liability.

Core Idea
Both rider and goader pay diyat when trampling occurs immediately, because their acts are independent causes, not condition and cause.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario in diyat law: a rider authorizes a goader to drive the animal, and the animal tramples a person. The key question is who is liable. The Hanafi ruling holds both liable if the trampling occurs during the goading, based on the principle that each act is an independent cause (illa) of death. The commentary uses a parallel example of wounding and falling into a well to illustrate the causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Read the passage to determine the two actions (riding and goading) and the stated hukm: both are jointly liable for diyat.
Focus on the condition of immediacy.
2
Understand the objection
The passage anticipates an objection: why is the goader liable when he acted under command? Recognize that the objector sees riding as the sole cause.
This sets up the distinction between cause and condition.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response explains that goading is not a condition (shart) of riding but rather a cause (illa) of movement, which is a separate cause of trampling. Thus two independent causes combine.
Note the temporal argument: goading occurs after riding, so it cannot be a condition for riding.
4
Examine the parallel case
The passage gives an analogy: a wounder and a well-digger both liable when the wounded falls into the well. This reinforces that multiple independent causes each trigger liability.
Use this to solidify the concept of multiple illas.
5
Apply the rule
Given a new scenario (e.g., different timing or multiple actors), decide liability by checking whether each act is an independent cause of death.
Remember: condition (shart) does not trigger liability; cause (illa) does.

تشریح اگر سوار نے خالد کو آر مارنے کی اجازت دی لہذا اس نے ماری اور چوپائے نے کسی کو روند دیا تو اب سوار اور ناخس دونوں ہے دیت واجب ہوگی لیکن یہ ضروری ہے کہ اس کے آر مارنے کی حالت میں چوپایہ کسی کو روندے ورنہ بعد میں روندنے کی صورت میں نخس کا اثر ختم ہونے کی وجہ سے خالد پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اور فی الفور مارنے کی صورت میں دونوں ضامن ہوں گے اس لئے کہ اس وقت چوپائے کا چلنا ان دونوں کی جانب مضاف ہوگا۔
سوال ناس نے جبکہ آررا کب کے حکم سے ماری ہے تو ناخس پر ضمان کیوں ہے؟ جواب زید نے خالد کو ہانکنے کی اجازت دی نہ کہ اختلاف کی۔ اس حیثیت کا تقاضہ تو یہ تھا کہ عمان صرف ناخس پر ہوتا مگر ہم نے نخس کے امر کی وجہ سے دونوں پر واجب کر دیا۔
سوال مقدر کا جواب
ولاركوب وان كان علة للوطى فالنخس ليس بشرط لهذه العلة بل هو شرط او علمة للسير والسير علة للوطى، وبهذا الا يترجح صاحب العلة
ترجمہ اور سوار ہونا اگر چه روندنے کی علت ہے پس آر مارنا اس ملت کی شردا نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ سیر کی شرط یا علت ہے اور سیر ولی کی علت ہے اور اسی وجہ سے صاحب علت راج نہیں ہے۔ تشریح یہ ایک اعتراض مقدر کا جواب ہے۔
اعتراض یہ ہے کہ راکب کا فعل روندنے کی علت ہے گویا کہ وہ خود اپنے پاؤں سے روند کر ہلاک کر رہا ہے اور ناخس کا فعل شرط ہے اور جب علت کے اندر صلاحیت ہو تو حکم کی اضافت شرط کی طرف نہیں ہوتی بلکہ علت کی طرف ہوتی ہے لہذا ضمان را کب پر ہونا چاہئے؟
جواب شرط تو پہلے ہوتی ہے اور یہاں نخس رکوب کے بعد ہے لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ کس علت (رکوب) کی شرط نہیں ہے بلکہ نفس تو سیر کی شرط یا علت ہے تو اب دو علتیں جمع ہو گئیں ایک نخس اور ایک رکوب ، لہذا ضمان دونوں پر ہوگا۔
الہزا ر کوب کو نس پر کوئی ترجیح نہ ہوگی ، اور ضمان میں دونوں برابر ہوں گے۔
مذکورہ مسئلہ کی نظیر
كمن جرح انساناً فوقع في بير حفرها غيره على قارعة الطريق و مات فالدية عليهما لما ان الحفر شرط علة
اخرى دون علة الجرح كذا هذا
ترجمہ جیسے کسی نے کسی انسان کو زخمی کیا پس وہ زخمی اس کنویں میں گر گیا جس کو جارح کے غیر نے شارع عام پر کھودا ہو، اور وہ مر گیا ہو تو دبیتہ ان دونوں پر ہوگی اس لئے کہ کنواں کھودنا دوسری ( وقوع ) علت کی شرط ہے نہ کہ زخمی کرنے کی ایسے ہی یہ ہے۔ تشریح زید نے عمر و کوزخمی کر دیا اور وہ اضطراب کی وجہ سے کنویں میں گر گیا جس کو خالد نے شارع عام پر کھودا ہے اور اب عمر ومر گیا تو
ختمان زید اور خالد دونوں پر آئے گا۔
کیونکہ جرح اور حفر دونوں ہی موت کا سبب ہیں تو یہ اول کے مثل ہو گیا، نیز حفر وقوع کی علت ہے جرح کی علت نہیں ہے۔
(كمالا يخفى)
Commentary: If the rider authorized Khalid to goad (the animal), so he goaded and the animal trampled someone, then both the rider and the goader owe blood money. But it is necessary that the animal tramples someone while he is goading; otherwise, if trampling occurs later, since the effect of goading has ceased, Khalid is not liable. In the case of immediate trampling, both are guarantors because the animal's movement at that time is attributed to both. Question: When the goader struck by the rider's command, why is he liable? Answer: Zaid authorized Khalid to drive, not to cause harm. This would require liability only on the goader, but we made both liable because of the command to goad. Answer to a presumed objection: 'And riding, even if it is the cause of trampling, goading is not a condition for this cause; rather it is a condition or cause for movement, and movement is the cause for trampling, and thus the owner of the cause is not preponderant.' Comment: This is the answer to a presumed objection. The objection is: The rider's act is the cause of trampling; he is as if he himself trampled and killed with his foot; the goader's act is a condition. When the cause has potential, the ruling is not attributed to the condition but to the cause, so liability should be on the rider. Answer: The condition precedes, but here the goading comes after the riding, so it is known that it is not a condition for the cause (riding) but rather a condition or cause for movement. Thus two causes combine: one goading, one riding, so liability is on both. Hence riding has no preponderance over goading, and they are equal in liability. A parallel to this issue: Like someone who wounds a person, and that wounded person falls into a well dug by another on the public road, and dies; then blood money is on both because digging is a condition for another cause (the fall) not for the cause of wounding. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya addresses a specific scenario in diyat (blood money) law: when a rider commands a goader to drive the animal, and the animal tramples someone. The ruling clarifies that both the rider and the goader are jointly liable for diyat if the trampling occurs immediately during the goading. The commentary explains the rationale: the goading is not a condition for the rider's act (riding) but rather a cause for the animal's movement, which in turn leads to trampling. Thus both acts are independent causes (illa) contributing to the death, so both pay diyat equally. A parallel example is given: a person wounds another, who then falls into a pit dug by a third party on a public road. Both the wounder and the digger are liable because each contributed to the death through separate causal chains. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that when multiple independent causes lead to death, all are liable, and the ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas.

Key Terms
ناخس (nākhis): The person who goads or drives the animal (often by poking or urging) at the rider's command.
راكب (rākib): The rider of the animal who authorizes the goading.
علة (ʻilla): Legal cause or effective reasoning that links an act to a ruling; here it refers to the act that directly leads to death (e.g., riding causing trampling).
شرط (sharṭ): Condition; an external factor that enables the cause to be effective but is not itself the cause (e.g., goading is a condition for movement).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the goader NOT liable for trampling?
A) If the trampling occurs immediately during goading
B) If the trampling occurs after the goading effect has ceased
C) If the rider did not authorize the goading
D) If the animal is a camel

Key Takeaway
Both rider and goader are jointly liable for diyat when the animal tramples immediately due to goading, as both acts are independent causes.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Can the Goader Recover from the Rider?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on goader's recourse against rider

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi opinions on the goader's right to recover diyat and state the preferred view with its rationale based on the separability of command and consequence.

Core Idea
Command to drive does not command trampling; a goader cannot recover from the rider.

After both the goader and rider are held jointly liable for diyat due to trampling, a subtle question arises: can the goader, who physically caused the harm, recover his share from the rider? The Hanafi school offers two opinions. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each, focusing on the key principle that a person is only liable for the specific act commanded, not for unintended separable consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the liability scenario
Both the goader (nakhis) and the rider (rakib) are jointly liable for the diyat of the person trampled. The question is whether the goader can seek reimbursement from the rider.
The goader acted by striking the animal; the rider commanded the driving.
2
State the first opinion
The first opinion holds that the goader can recover from the rider because he performed the act (goading) under the rider's command. The command implies responsibility for the outcome.
This opinion emphasizes the causal link between command and action.
3
State the second opinion (more correct)
The second opinion, declared as 'asah' (more correct) by the author, denies recovery. The rider only commanded driving (tasyir), not trampling (ita'). Goading and trampling are separable acts; the goader's action does not necessarily lead to trampling.
The key is the distinction between the commanded act and the unintended result.
4
Analyze the supporting analogy
The author draws an analogy: if someone commands a boy to drive an animal, and the animal tramples a person, the boy's 'aqila (agnatic group) are liable, but they cannot recover from the commander. This reinforces the principle that command to drive does not cover trampling.
The analogy illustrates that the commander is not liable for separable consequences.
5
Determine the practical thamarah
According to the preferred opinion, the goader bears the full diyat himself with no recourse against the rider. This affects how the liability is ultimately distributed.
If a judge applies the asah opinion, the goader cannot shift any part of the payment.

Source Text

سوال نخس کا حکم ایطاء کا حکم ہے؟ جواب غلط ہے کیونکہ یہ بات اس وقت درست ہوتی ہے کہ نخس ایطاء کو مستلزم ہوتا ، حالانکہ ایسی بات نہیں ہے کیونکہ ان دونوں میں انفصال ہے۔
مسئلہ مذکورہ کے شواہد
وصار كما اذا امر صبيًّا يستمسك على الدابة بتيسيرها فوطئت انساناً ومات حتى ضمن عاقلة الصبر فانهم لا يرجعون على الأمر لانه امره بالتسيير والايطاء ينفصل عنه ترجمہ اور ایسے ہو گیا جیسے کسی بچہ کو حکم کیا ہو جو چو پائ پر بیٹھ سکتا ہے چوپائے کو چلانے کا اپس اس نے کسی انسان کو روند دیا اور وہ مر گیا۔ یہاں تک کہ بچہ کے عاقلہ ضامن ہوئے تو یہ آمر پر رجوع نہیں کریں گے۔ اس لئے کہ اس نے بچہ کو چلانے کا حکم کیا ہے اور روند ناتسییر منفصل ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح حکم اول کے کچھ شواہد پیش فرما رہے ہیں زید نے ایک ایسے بچہ کو جو چوپائے پر تم سکتا ہے اس کو چلانے کا حکم کیا لہذا بچہ نے چلایا اور چوپائے نے کسی کو روند کر مار دیا تو بچہ کے عاقلہ پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ لیکن اب وہ آمر سے یہ ضمان واپس لینے کے حقدار نہ ہوں
Will the goader recover from the rider or not? Then it is said: The goader may recover from the rider the amount he paid as guarantee for the trampling, because he acted by his command. And it is said: He does not recover, and this is the more correct in my opinion, because the rider did not command him to trample, and the goading is separable from the trampling. Translation: It was said that the goader will recover from the rider the amount he guaranteed in the case of trampling because he acted by his command. And it was said that he will not recover, and this is the more correct in my opinion, because the rider did not command him to trample, and goading is separable from it. Commentary: In any case, in the mentioned scenario, liability is on both the goader and the rider. Then does the goader have the right to recover from the rider? There are two opinions: (1) He will recover, because the goader acted by the rider's command. (2) He will not recover, which the author considers more correct, giving the reason that the rider commanded him to drive, not to trample, and here trampling occurred. Question: Is the command to goad equivalent to the command to trample? Answer: No, because that would be true only if goading necessarily leads to trampling, but it does not; they are separable. Evidence for the mentioned issue: It is like when someone commands a boy who is able to sit on an animal to drive it, and it tramples someone and dies, so the boy's 'aqila (agnatic group) pay the diyat. They do not recover from the commander because he commanded driving, and trampling is separate from it.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage discusses a subsidiary question: after both the goader and rider are jointly liable for diyat, can the goader recover his share from the rider? The author presents two opinions within the Hanafi school. The first opinion allows recovery because the goader acted under the rider's command. The second, considered more correct (asah) by the author (possibly al-Marghinani in Hidayah), denies recovery. The reasoning is that the rider commanded only the act of driving (tasyir), not the trampling (ita'), and the two are separable. The goading does not necessarily lead to trampling. The author supports this with a parallel case: if someone commands a boy to drive an animal and it tramples someone, the boy's 'aqila are liable and cannot recover from the commander. This illustrates the principle that a person is only liable for the specific act commanded, not for unintended consequences that are not inherently tied to the command.

Key Terms
إبطاء (īṭāʼ): The act of trampling or causing the animal to step on someone, resulting in death or injury.
رجوع (rujūʻ): Legal recourse or claim for reimbursement against another co-liable party.
أصح (aṣaḥḥ): The more correct opinion among two or more views within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the more correct opinion (asah) in the Hanafi school, can the goader recover the diyat he paid from the rider if both were liable?
A) Yes, because he acted by the rider's command
B) No, because the rider did not command the trampling
C) Yes, but only half
D) No, because the goader is solely liable

Key Takeaway
The goader cannot recover from the rider because the command to drive does not imply command to trample; the acts are separable.

Right of Recourse for the Proder (Nakhis) in Trampling Cases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on recourse of proder against rider in trampling cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on whether the nakhis can recover from the rider for trampling compensation and identify which is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
A command to drive does not include permission to prod; separate acts bear separate liability.

When a rider orders a driver to move the animal, and the driver prods it causing trampling, both may be liable. But can the driver recover the compensation from the rider? Hanafi scholars disagree. This lesson unpacks the two qawls, the analogies used to support the preferred view, and why the author declares the second qawl as the most correct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two qawls
Read the first part of the passage to find the two opinions: one says the nakhis recovers from the rider because he acted by command; the other says he does not because the rider only commanded driving, not prodding.
The author clearly labels them as 'قيل' and 'قيل'.
2
Analyze the evidence for each qawl
For the first qawl, the basis is that the nakhis acted by the rider's command. For the second, the rider's command was specific to driving, and prodding is a separate act that does not necessarily lead to trampling.
The second qawl relies on the concept of 'infisal' (separation) between the commanded act and the harmful result.
3
Examine the first analogy (child driving)
The passage analogizes to a child ordered to drive an animal: if the animal tramples someone, the child's 'aqila pays but cannot recover from the commander. This shows that command to drive does not entail liability for trampling.
The illa is that driving does not necessarily cause trampling, just as prodding does not necessarily cause trampling.
4
Examine the second analogy (weapon to child)
Similarly, if a child is given a weapon and kills someone, the child is liable and cannot recover from the giver. This reinforces that the giver's command or provision does not automatically transfer liability for harmful acts.
Both analogies are qiyas khafi and support the second qawl.
5
Determine the tarjih and its basis
The author declares the second qawl as the most correct (asahh) and explains that the rider's command was for driving, not prodding, and the two are separate. Thus, the mufta bihi is that the nakhis cannot recover.
This is a fatawa mutakhkhirin position, not from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

وصار كما اذا امر صبيًّا يستمسك على الدابة بتيسيرها فوطئت انساناً ومات حتى ضمن عاقلة الصبر فانهم لا يرجعون على الأمر لانه امره بالتسيير والايطاء ينفصل عنه ترجمہ اور ایسے ہو گیا جیسے کسی بچہ کو حکم کیا ہو جو چو پائ پر بیٹھ سکتا ہے چوپائے کو چلانے کا اپس اس نے کسی انسان کو روند دیا اور وہ مر گیا۔ یہاں تک کہ بچہ کے عاقلہ ضامن ہوئے تو یہ آمر پر رجوع نہیں کریں گے۔ اس لئے کہ اس نے بچہ کو چلانے کا حکم کیا ہے اور روند ناتسییر منفصل ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح حکم اول کے کچھ شواہد پیش فرما رہے ہیں زید نے ایک ایسے بچہ کو جو چوپائے پر تم سکتا ہے اس کو چلانے کا حکم کیا لہذا بچہ نے چلایا اور چوپائے نے کسی کو روند کر مار دیا تو بچہ کے عاقلہ پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ لیکن اب وہ آمر سے یہ ضمان واپس لینے کے حقدار نہ ہوں گے ۔ اس لئے کہ چلا نا روند نے کو مستلزم نہیں ہے جیسے نخس روند نے کو مستلزم نہیں ہے۔ بچہ کے ہاتھ میں ہتھیار تھما دیا جس سے کوئی مرگیا تو بچہ پر ضمان ہے، ناخس پر کب ضمان ہے اور کب نہیں وكذا اذا ناولــــه ســلاحــاً فــقتــل بــــه اخر حتى ضمن لا يـــرجــع عـلـى الأمــــر ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی جب کہ بچہ کو ہتھیار دے دیا پس اس نے اس سے کسی دوسرے کو قتل کر دیا یہاں تک کہ وہ ضامن ہوا تو وہ آمر پر رجوع نہیں کرے گا۔ تشریح بچہ کے ہاتھ میں ہتھیار پکڑا دیا جس سے کوئی مر گیا اور بچہ پر ضمان واجب ہوا تو اب اس ضمان کو آمر سے واپس نہیں لیا جائے گا۔
Will the proder take liability from the rider or not? Then it was said: The proder recovers from the rider the amount he was liable for in the trampling, because he acted by his command. And it was said: He does not recover, and this is more correct in my opinion, because the rider did not command him to trample, and the prodding is separate from the trampling. Translation: Then it was said that the proder will seek recourse from the rider for the amount he became liable for in the case of trampling, because he did it by the rider's command. And it was said that he will not seek recourse, and this is the most correct according to my suspicion, because the rider did not order him to trample, and the prodding is separate from the trampling. Explanation: In any case, in the mentioned scenario, liability is obligatory on both the proder and the rider. Then, does the proder have the right to take back from the rider? There are two sayings: 1) He will take back, because what the proder did was by the rider's command. 2) He will not take back, and the author has declared this as the most correct. He gave the proof that the rider commanded him to drive, not to trample, and here trampling occurred. Question: Is the rule of prodding the rule of trampling? Answer: Incorrect, because that would be correct only if prodding entailed trampling, but that is not the case because there is separation between them. Evidence for the mentioned issue: And it is as if he ordered a child who can sit on the animal to drive it, then it trampled a person and died, so the child's 'aqila became liable, and they do not seek recourse from the commander, because he ordered the driving, and trampling is separate from it. Translation: And it became like when a child was ordered who can sit on the animal to drive it, and he drove, and the animal trampled a person and he died, so the child's 'aqila are liable, then they do not seek recourse from the commander, because he ordered the driving and trampling is separate from driving. Explanation: He is presenting some evidence for the first ruling. Zayd ordered a child who can sit on the animal to drive it, so the child drove, and the animal trampled and killed someone, so the child's 'aqila are liable for the blood money. But they are not entitled to recover this from the commander, because driving does not entail trampling, just as prodding does not entail trampling. And similarly if he handed a weapon to the child and he killed another with it, so he is liable, and he does not seek recourse from the commander. Translation: And similarly, when he gave a weapon to a child, then he killed another with it, so he is liable, and he does not seek recourse from the commander. Explanation: The child was handed a weapon which caused someone's death, and the child's liability was established, then this liability will not be recovered from the commander.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the person who prods an animal (nakhis) can recover the compensation he paid from the rider (raakib) when both are liable for the animal's trampling. The author presents two qawls: the first says yes because the nakhis acted by the rider's command; the second says no because the rider only commanded driving, not prodding, and the prodding is a separate act that does not necessarily cause trampling. The author declares the second as the most correct. To support this, he gives two analogies: first, if a child is ordered to drive an animal and it tramples someone, the child's 'aqila is liable but cannot recover from the commander. Second, if a child is given a weapon and kills someone, the child is liable and cannot recover from the giver. Both analogies show that the act of driving or giving a weapon does not necessarily entail the harmful result (trampling or killing), just as prodding does not entail trampling. Therefore, the nakhis cannot recourse against the rider.

Key Terms
الناخس (an-nākhis): The person who prods or pokes the animal, causing it to move or bolt.
الراكب (ar-rākib): The rider of the animal.
الإيطاء (al-īṭā'): Causing the animal to trample or tread upon a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, can the proder (nakhis) recover from the rider for the trampling compensation?
A) Yes, because he acted by command.
B) No, because the command was to drive, not to prod.
C) Only if the rider explicitly ordered prodding.
D) Only if the trampling was immediate.

Key Takeaway
A command to drive does not imply command to prod; therefore the proder cannot recover from the rider for trampling compensation.

Condition for Nakhis Liability: Immediate Trampling After Prodding
Ruling on liability for trampling after prodding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the proder's liability and apply it to cases involving a rider, leader, or driver.

Core Idea
Prodder liable only if trampling is immediate; otherwise rider bears liability.

This lesson clarifies a nuanced ruling in diyat: when an animal is prodded and tramples someone, who bears the liability? The answer hinges on timing. The Hanafi school specifies that only if the harm occurs immediately after the prodding is the proder (nakhis) responsible. Otherwise, the rider remains liable. We will examine the legal reasoning and extend the rule to situations where a leader or driver is involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Recognize the actors: the proder (nakhis), the rider (raakib), the leader (qaa'id), and the driver (saa'iq). The act is prodding an animal leading to trampling.
2
State the condition for nakhis liability
According to the text, the nakhis is liable only if the trampling occurs immediately after prodding (fawr). Understand 'fawr' as an unbroken sequence.
If trampling is not immediate, liability shifts to the rider.
3
Apply to the rider scenario
If a rider is on the animal and someone prods it, the rider is liable unless the trampling is immediate. The effect of prodding is cut off after a delay.
4
Extend to leader/driver scenarios
The same rule applies if a leader or driver is controlling the animal and a third party prods it. If immediate trampling occurs, the prodder is liable; otherwise, the leader/driver is liable.
The text explicitly covers qaa'id and saa'iq cases.
5
Formulate the general principle
The liability follows the cause: whoever's action is the direct and immediate cause of the harm is liable. If the effect is broken, attribution returns to the original controller.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on causality in torts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الناخس إنما يضمن إذا كان الإيطاء في فور النخس حتى يكون السوق مضافا إليه وإذا لم يكن في فور ذالک فالضمان على الراكب لانقطاع أثر النخس فبقى السوق مضافا إلى الراكب على الكمال و من قاد دابة فنخسها رجل فانفلتت من يد القائد فأصابت فى فورها فهو على الناخس وكذا إذا كان لها سائق فنخسها غيره لأنه مضاف إليه ترجمہ پھر ناخس ضامن ہو گا جب کہ روند نانخس کے علی الفور ہو۔ یہاں تک کہ سوق ناخس کی جانب مضاف ہو اور جب کہ روند نا فورانس کے بعد نہ ہو تو منمان را کب پر ہو گا خس کا اثر منقطع ہونے کی وجہ سے پس سوق مضاف باقی رہا مکمل طریقہ پر را کب کی جانب۔ تشریح نخس کے فورا بعد روند نا اگر پایا جائے تو ناخس پر ضمان ہوگا۔ ورنہ پھر ضمان را کب پر ہوگا۔ قائد یا سائق ہو اور ناخس نے چوپایہ کو چھیڑ دیا جس سے علی الفور کوئی مر گیا تو ضمان ناخس پر ہے و من قاد دابة فنخسها رجل فانفلتت من يد القائد فأصابت فى فورها فهو على الناخس وكذا إذا كان لها سائق فنخسها غيره لأنه مضاف إليه تشریح بجائے راکب کے اگر قائد یا سائق نے یا اور کسی نے چوپائے کو آر مار دی جس نے علی الفور کوئی مرگیا تو ضمان ناخس پر ہو گا اس لئے کہ چوپائے کا فعل ناخنس ہی کی جانب مضاف ہوگا۔
Then the proder is only liable if the trampling is immediately after the prodding, so that the driving is attributed to him. And if it is not immediate, then liability is on the rider because the effect of the prodding is cut off, so the driving remains attributed to the rider completely. Translation: Then the proder is liable only when the trampling is immediately after the prodding, so the driving is attributed to him, and when it is not immediate, the liability is on the rider because the effect of the prodding is cut off. Explanation: If trampling is found immediately after prodding, the proder is liable; otherwise, the rider is liable. Whether the animal has a leader or driver, if someone prods it and it immediately causes a death, liability is on the proder. And if a man leads an animal and another prods it, and it slips from the leader's hand and hits someone immediately, it is on the proder. Similarly, if it has a driver and another prods it, because it is attributed to him. Translation: And if someone led an animal, and another prodded it, and it got loose from the leader's hand and hit someone immediately, then the liability is on the proder. Similarly, if it had a driver and another prodded it, because the action of the animal is attributed to him. Explanation: Instead of the rider, if the leader or driver or another person prodded the animal and immediately someone died, the proder is liable because the animal's action is attributed to the proder.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling on when the proder (nakhis) is liable for an animal's trampling. The key condition is immediacy (fawr): if the trampling occurs right after the prodding, the nakhis is liable; otherwise, the rider (raakib) is liable. The rationale is that the effect of the prodding is cut off after a delay, so the animal's action remains attributed to the rider. The passage then applies this rule to cases where the animal is being led (qaa'id) or driven (saa'iq) by someone other than the rider. If a third party prods the animal and it immediately tramples someone, that third party is liable, not the leader or driver.

Key Terms
الفور (al-fawr): Immediately, without delay; the condition for the nakhis's liability is that the trampling occurs instantly after the prodding.
القائد (al-qā'id): The person who leads the animal, holding its halter or rope.
السائق (as-sā'iq): The person who drives the animal from behind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only if the trampling occurs immediately after the prodding.
C) Only if he is the rider.
D) Only if the animal has no leader or driver.

Key Takeaway
The proder is liable only if the trampling is immediate; otherwise, the original controller (rider, leader, driver) bears liability.
